<p>The <b>white-tailed eagle</b> (<i>Haliaeetus albicilla</i>) is a very large species of <a href="/wiki/Sea_eagle" title="Sea eagle">sea eagle</a> widely distributed across temperate <a href="/wiki/Palearctic" class="mw-redirect" title="Palearctic">Eurasia</a>.  As are all eagles, it is a member of the family <a href="/wiki/Accipitridae" title="Accipitridae">Accipitridae</a> (or accipitrids) which includes other diurnal <a href="/wiki/Raptor_(bird)" class="mw-redirect" title="Raptor (bird)">raptors</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Hawk" title="Hawk">hawks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kite_(bird)" title="Kite (bird)">kites</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Harrier_(bird)" title="Harrier (bird)">harriers</a>. One of up to eleven members in the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Sea_eagle" title="Sea eagle">Haliaeetus</a></i>, which are commonly called sea eagles, it is also referred to as the <b>white-tailed sea-eagle</b>.<sup id="cite_ref-Helander_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Helander-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Sometimes, it is known as the <b>ern</b> or <b>erne</b> (depending on spelling by sources),<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> <b>gray sea eagle</b><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> and <b>Eurasian sea eagle</b><sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>While found across a very wide range, today breeding as far west as <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a> across to as far east in <a href="/wiki/Hokkaido" title="Hokkaido">Hokkaido</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>, they are often scarce and very spottily distributed as a nesting species, mainly due to human activities. These have included habitat alterations and destruction of wetlands, about a hundred years of systematic persecution by humans (from the early 1800s to around <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>) followed by inadvertent poisonings and epidemics of nesting failures due to various manmade chemical pesticides and organic compounds, which have threatened eagles since roughly the 1950s and continue to be a potential concern. Due to this, the white-tailed eagle was considered endangered or extinct in several countries.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hailer_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hailer-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Krone_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Krone-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> However, some populations have recovered well due to some governmental protections and dedicated conservationists and naturalists protecting habitats and nesting sites and partially regulating poaching and pesticide usage, as well as careful reintroductions into parts of their former range.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Krüger_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Krüger-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Love2_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love2-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>White-tailed eagles usually live most of the year near large bodies of open water, including both coastal saltwater areas and inland freshwater, and require an abundant food supply and old-growth trees or ample sea cliffs for nesting.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> They are considered a close cousin of the <a href="/wiki/Bald_eagle" title="Bald eagle">bald eagle</a> (<i>Haliaeetus leucocephalus</i>), which occupies a similar <a href="/wiki/Ecological_niche" title="Ecological niche">niche</a> in North America.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWinkHeidrichFentzloff1996_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWinkHeidrichFentzloff1996-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxonomy">Taxonomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=White-tailed_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Taxonomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The first <a href="/wiki/Species_description" title="Species description">formal description</a> of the white-tailed eagle was by the Swedish naturalist <a href="/wiki/Carl_Linnaeus" title="Carl Linnaeus">Carl Linnaeus</a> in 1758 in the <a href="/wiki/10th_edition_of_Systema_Naturae" title="10th edition of Systema Naturae">tenth edition</a> of his <i><a href="/wiki/Systema_Naturae" title="Systema Naturae">Systema Naturae</a></i> under the <a href="/wiki/Binomial_nomenclature" title="Binomial nomenclature">binomial name</a> <i>Falco albicilla</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">genus</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Haliaeetus" class="mw-redirect" title="Haliaeetus">Haliaeetus</a></i> was introduced in 1809 by the French naturalist <a href="/wiki/Marie_Jules_C%C3%A9sar_Savigny" title="Marie Jules César Savigny">Marie Jules César Savigny</a> in the <i><a href="/wiki/Description_de_l%27%C3%89gypte" title="Description de l&#39;Égypte">Description de l'Égypte</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> The name <i>Haliaeetus</i> is <a href="/wiki/New_Latin" title="New Latin">New Latin</a> for "sea-eagle", from <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Ancient Greek</a> <i>hali-</i>, "sea-" and <i>aetos</i>, "eagle". The specific <i>albicilla</i>, "white-tailed", is from New Latin <i>albi-</i>, "white" and <i>cilla</i>, "tail".<sup id="cite_ref-Jobling_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jobling-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglo-Saxon">Anglo-Saxon</a> name <i>erne</i> means “soarer”.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> It has many Gaelic names, including <i>iolar sùil na grèine</i> or 'eagle of the sun's eye.'<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThe_Nature_of_Scotland49_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThe_Nature_of_Scotland49-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Systematics">Systematics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=White-tailed_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Systematics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:White-tailed_Sea-Eagle_-_Hokkaido_-_Japan_S4E9284_(15437353795).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/White-tailed_Sea-Eagle_-_Hokkaido_-_Japan_S4E9284_%2815437353795%29.jpg/220px-White-tailed_Sea-Eagle_-_Hokkaido_-_Japan_S4E9284_%2815437353795%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/White-tailed_Sea-Eagle_-_Hokkaido_-_Japan_S4E9284_%2815437353795%29.jpg/330px-White-tailed_Sea-Eagle_-_Hokkaido_-_Japan_S4E9284_%2815437353795%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/White-tailed_Sea-Eagle_-_Hokkaido_-_Japan_S4E9284_%2815437353795%29.jpg/440px-White-tailed_Sea-Eagle_-_Hokkaido_-_Japan_S4E9284_%2815437353795%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1140" data-file-height="760" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:White-tailed_Sea-Eagle_-_Hokkaido_-_Japan_S4E9284_(15437353795).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Wintering adult white-tailed eagle in <a href="/wiki/Hokkaido" title="Hokkaido">Hokkaido</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The white-tailed eagle is a member of the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Sea_eagle" title="Sea eagle">Haliaeetus</a></i>, a <a href="/wiki/Monophyly" title="Monophyly">monophylic group</a> comprised by 11 living species, including the closely related <i>Ichthyophaga</i> fish eagles which may or may not be part of a separate genus. The latter group, comprised by the <a href="/wiki/Lesser_fish_eagle" title="Lesser fish eagle">lesser</a> (<i>Haliaeetus humilis</i>) and the <a href="/wiki/Grey-headed_fish_eagle" title="Grey-headed fish eagle">grey-headed fish eagle</a> (<i>Haliaeetus ichthyaetus</i>), differ mostly in life history, being more fully devoted to fish eating and habituating wooded areas, especially in mountainous areas. In appearance the two <i>Ichthyaetus</i> are slenderer, longer tailed and more uniform and grey in colour than typical sea eagles. This species pair may not be genetically distinct enough to warrant division into separate genera.<sup id="cite_ref-Seibold_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Seibold-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Griffiths_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Griffiths-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Other than these <i>Ichthyophaga</i>-type species found farther north in Asia, <a href="/wiki/Sanford%27s_sea_eagle" title="Sanford&#39;s sea eagle">Sanford's sea eagle</a> (<i>Haliaeetus sanfordi</i>) of the <a href="/wiki/Solomon_Islands" title="Solomon Islands">Solomon Islands</a> is the most atypical <i>Haliaeetus</i>, retaining rufous-brown plumage into adulthood (this particularly resembling the <a href="/wiki/White-bellied_sea_eagle" title="White-bellied sea eagle">white-bellied sea eagle</a> juvenile, likely a closely related species) more typical of juveniles in other species and it also dwells more so in dense, coastal forests where it feeds mostly on birds and mammals rather than <a href="/wiki/Fish" title="Fish">fish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Water_bird" title="Water bird">water birds</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Outside of the genus <i>Haliaeetus</i>, among other extant forms, they appear to be most closely related to <a href="/wiki/Milvinae" title="Milvinae">milvine kites</a> and <a href="/wiki/Old_World_vulture" title="Old World vulture">Old World vultures</a>, based on modern forms from these subfamilies that broadly share morphological and life history traits with sea eagles: the <a href="/wiki/Brahminy_kite" title="Brahminy kite">Brahminy kite</a> (<i>Haliastur indus</i>) (historically sometimes referred to as the “red-backed sea eagle”) and the <a href="/wiki/Palm-nut_vulture" title="Palm-nut vulture">palm-nut vulture</a> (<i>Gypohierax angolensis</i>) (which was once widely referred to as the “vulturine fish eagle”). The relation of these species to the sea eagles is partially borne out by their genetic sequencing.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wink2_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wink2-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Other groups, beyond milvine kites and Old World vulture, of modern accipitrid that are seemingly in some way related, albeit very distantly, to the sea eagles include <i><a href="/wiki/Accipiter" title="Accipiter">Accipiters</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Harrier_(bird)" title="Harrier (bird)">harriers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Melierax" title="Melierax">chanting-goshawks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Buteoninae" title="Buteoninae">buteonines</a>. Notably excluded from their relations are most other species referred to as “eagles”, including <a href="/wiki/Aquilinae" title="Aquilinae">booted eagles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Circaetinae" title="Circaetinae">snake and serpent eagles</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_9-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWinkHeidrichFentzloff1996_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWinkHeidrichFentzloff1996-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wink2_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wink2-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The white-tailed eagle itself forms a <a href="/wiki/Cryptic_species_complex" class="mw-redirect" title="Cryptic species complex">species pair</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Bald_eagle" title="Bald eagle">bald eagle</a>. These diverged from other sea eagles at the beginning of the early <a href="/wiki/Miocene" title="Miocene">Miocene</a> (c. 10&#160;<a href="/wiki/Mya_(unit)" class="mw-redirect" title="Mya (unit)">mya</a>) at the latest, possibly (if the most ancient fossil record is correctly assigned to this genus) as early as the early or middle <a href="/wiki/Oligocene" title="Oligocene">Oligocene</a>, about 28&#160;<a href="/wiki/Mya_(unit)" class="mw-redirect" title="Mya (unit)">mya</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWinkHeidrichFentzloff1996_12-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWinkHeidrichFentzloff1996-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> A recent genetic study of <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrial_DNA" title="Mitochondrial DNA">mitochondrial DNA</a><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHailerHelanderFolkestadGanusevich2007_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHailerHelanderFolkestadGanusevich2007-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> is consistent with this idea. Greenlandic white-tailed eagles (proposed as <i>H. a. groenlandicus</i>) form, on <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolutionary</a> time scales, a relatively recently founded population that has not yet accumulated many unique genetic characteristics and may not strictly fulfill the distinction of a subspecies. However, the population appears to be demographically isolated and deserves special protection. At one time an eastern subspecies (<i>H. a. brooksi</i>) was proposed as well but there is little evidence supporting this as more than a case of clinal variation in coloring and size (i.e. the eastern average slightly darker and smaller than more westerly ones).<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> As in other sea-eagle species pairs, this one consists of a white-headed (the bald eagle) and a tan-headed species. They probably diverged in the North <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific</a>, spreading westwards into Eurasia and eastwards into <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a>. Like the third large northern species, <a href="/wiki/Steller%27s_sea_eagle" title="Steller&#39;s sea eagle">Steller's sea eagle</a> (<i>Haliaeetus pelagicus</i>), adults have yellow feet, beaks and eyes. Another species, likely intermediate between the white-tailed, bald and Steller's sea eagles and the <i>Ichthyophaga</i> type fish eagles, is the <a href="/wiki/Pallas%27s_fish_eagle" title="Pallas&#39;s fish eagle">Pallas's fish eagle</a>, which in life history seems to range farther from water and to higher elevations than the three northern species normally do. Due to the similar dietary and nesting habits of sea eagles, they are mostly <a href="/wiki/Allopatric_speciation" title="Allopatric speciation">allopatric</a> in distribution as competition can be considerable between these eagles.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Seibold_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Seibold-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Currently, eagles only occur in the <a href="/wiki/Hawaiian_Islands" title="Hawaiian Islands">Hawaiian Islands</a> as vagrants, but Quaternary bones of <i><a href="/wiki/Haliaeetus" class="mw-redirect" title="Haliaeetus">Haliaeetus</a></i> have been found on three of the major islands. An ancient DNA study published in 2015 characterized the rapidly evolving mitochondrial control region of one of these specimens.<sup id="cite_ref-Hailer2015_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hailer2015-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> from a ∼3500-year-old sea eagle skeleton found in a lava cave on <a href="/wiki/Maui" title="Maui">Maui</a> was sequenced. Phylogenetic analyses suggested that the Hawaiian eagle represents a distinct (&gt;3% divergent) mtDNA lineage that is most closely related to extant white-tailed eagles. Based on fossil calibration, the Hawaiian mtDNA lineage probably diverged around the Middle Pleistocene. Thus, although not clearly differentiated in morphology from its relatives, the Hawaiian eagle likely represented an isolated, resident population in the Hawaiian archipelago for more than 100,000 years, where it was the largest terrestrial predator. The reasons for its extinction are unknown.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Description">Description</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=White-tailed_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Description">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Size">Size</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=White-tailed_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Size">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:White_tailed_eagle.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/White_tailed_eagle.jpg/220px-White_tailed_eagle.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="315" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/White_tailed_eagle.jpg/330px-White_tailed_eagle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/White_tailed_eagle.jpg/440px-White_tailed_eagle.jpg 2x" data-file-width="557" data-file-height="797" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:White_tailed_eagle.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The white-tailed eagle is the largest eagle found in Europe and most of its Asian range.</div></div></div>
<p>The white-tailed eagle is a very large bird and one of the largest living birds of prey. It is the largest of the dozen species called eagle to be found in Europe and is the largest eagle across its distribution, excluding the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Far_East" title="Russian Far East">Russian Far East</a> and during winter in <a href="/wiki/Hokkaido" title="Hokkaido">Hokkaido</a> where it co-exists with its larger cousin, <a href="/wiki/Steller%27s_sea_eagle" title="Steller&#39;s sea eagle">Steller's sea eagle</a>. The white-tailed eagle is sometimes considered the fourth largest eagle in the world <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThe_Nature_of_Scotland49_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThe_Nature_of_Scotland49-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> and is on average the fourth heaviest eagle in the world. The only extant eagle species known to be more massive in mean bulk are Steller's sea eagle, the <a href="/wiki/Harpy_eagle" title="Harpy eagle">harpy eagle</a> (<i>Harpia harpyja</i>) and the <a href="/wiki/Philippine_eagle" title="Philippine eagle">Philippine eagle</a> (<i>Pithecophaga jefferyi</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_9-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-HBW_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>  The white-tailed eagle measures anywhere from 66 to 94&#160;cm (26 to 37&#160;in) in total length with a typical wingspan of 1.78 to 2.45&#160;m (5&#160;ft 10&#160;in to 8&#160;ft 0&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_9-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NatlGeog_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NatlGeog-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> This species may have the largest wingspan of any living eagle.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_9-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-HBW_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Steller%27s_sea_eagle" title="Steller&#39;s sea eagle">Steller's sea eagle</a>, which is larger in weight, total length and non-wing standard measurements, may be the closest rival for median wingspan amongst living eagles.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_9-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>  Average wingspans are not known for the Steller's species, however white-tailed eagles do appear to outsize the average wingspan of the <a href="/wiki/Wedge-tailed_eagle" title="Wedge-tailed eagle">wedge-tailed eagle</a> (<i>Aquila audax</i>), which is sometimes also titled the largest winged extant eagle (however one wedge-tail did possess the largest wingspan ever verified for an extant eagle).<sup id="cite_ref-Wood_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wood-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> In one sample from <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a>, 5 male white-tailed eagle were found to average 2.26&#160;m (7&#160;ft 5&#160;in) and 8 females were found to average 2.37&#160;m (7&#160;ft 9&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Willgohs_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willgohs-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> In another sample of wild birds of unspecified origin, 5 males were found to average 2.1&#160;m (6&#160;ft 11&#160;in) and 7 females averaged 2.3&#160;m (7&#160;ft 7&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Record wingspans have included a specimen from Greenland which measured 2.53&#160;m (8&#160;ft 4&#160;in) while another specimen apparently spanned 2.6&#160;m (8&#160;ft 6&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-ravingravens_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ravingravens-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> The bald eagle broadly overlaps in size with the white-tailed eagle. In direct comparison, the white-tailed eagle averages somewhat larger in body mass than the bald eagle and may be marginally larger in bill and talon size although these linear aspect can be quite similar between the two species. However, the white-tailed has a significantly larger <a href="/wiki/Wing_chord_(biology)" title="Wing chord (biology)">wing chord</a> and average wingspan. On the other hand, the bald eagle usually possesses a longer tail length on average, which imparts a somewhat longer total length that the white-tailed eagle, and a longer mean tarsal length.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_9-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Friedmann_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Friedmann-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Imler_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Imler-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Size variation is generally a <a href="/wiki/Cline_(biology)" title="Cline (biology)">clinal</a> trend, these figures usually being measured in standard measurement such as wing, tail and tarsal length, or body mass rather than wingspan or total length. As expected for many widely distributed animals of varied lineages, the white-tailed eagle conforms to <a href="/wiki/Bergmann%27s_rule" title="Bergmann&#39;s rule">Bergmann's rule</a> in that more northerly birds tend to outsize those found relatively closer to the <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">Equator</a>. Somewhat less predictably, size seems to decrease from west to the east as well.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHailerHelanderFolkestadGanusevich2007_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHailerHelanderFolkestadGanusevich2007-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Zhengjie_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zhengjie-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> The largest white-tailed eagles appear to be found in <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a>, which are just slightly larger than those from <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavia</a> and notably larger than eagles from <a href="/wiki/Central_Europe" title="Central Europe">central Europe</a>, especially in proportions of the wing area. Meanwhile, those from the southerly portions of their breeding range, such as <a href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" class="mw-redirect" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a> (principally <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>), southern <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Korea_Bay" title="Korea Bay">Korea Bay</a> appear to be the smallest-bodied population, but this is complicated by the fact that there's nearly been no comprehensive measurements or published weights known for these extremely sporadic and rare Asian populations of eagle. Furthermore, weights of fully grown eagles from Greenland are not known.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_9-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brown_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Unlike many accipitrids, in white-tailed eagle (and seemingly other sea eagles as well) juveniles are often of similar weight to adult eagles, whereas in most the juveniles will usually weigh somewhat less. However, more typically, juvenile eagles have somewhat larger average wing and tail lengths than adults.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cramp_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cramp-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> In the white-tailed eagle, body mass can  typically range from 4 to 6.9&#160;kg (8.8 to 15.2&#160;lb) in females. The slightly smaller male may typically weigh from 3.1 to 5.4&#160;kg (6.8 to 11.9&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_9-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Average weights in European white-tailed eagles can range from 4.02&#160;kg (8.9&#160;lb) in 5 males and 5.11&#160;kg (11.3&#160;lb) in 9 females to (from the reintroduced birds of Scotland of Norwegian stock) 4.98&#160;kg (11.0&#160;lb) in 39 males and 6.06&#160;kg (13.4&#160;lb) in 43 females.<sup id="cite_ref-Love2_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love2-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brown_32-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> In comparison, the weight ranges for white-tailed eagles from <a href="/wiki/Northeast_China" title="Northeast China">northeast China</a> were claimed as only 2.8 to 3.78&#160;kg (6.2 to 8.3&#160;lb) in males and 3.75 to 4.6&#160;kg (8.3 to 10.1&#160;lb) in females.<sup id="cite_ref-Zhengjie_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zhengjie-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> The heaviest female white-tailed eagles can apparently scale up to 7.5–8&#160;kg (17–18&#160;lb) and even males can sometimes weigh up to 6.5&#160;kg (14&#160;lb), which would make the largest males perhaps the heaviest recorded modern male eagle as male harpy and Philippine eagles (being more sexual dimorphic in favor of the female) are not known to exceed 5&#160;kg (11&#160;lb) (the highest weights for male Steller's sea eagle are not known).<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wood_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wood-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CRC_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> The global mean body mass of white-tailed eagles is estimated at approximately 5&#160;kg (11&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Nadjafzadeh_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nadjafzadeh-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> The average female Steller's sea eagle may weigh just under 25% more than the average female white-tailed eagle (the average weight of male Steller's is not known) while the average European golden eagle weigh about 11-12% less than the average European white-tailed eagle and the bald eagle species as a whole about 10% less than the white-tailed eagle species.<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_39-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Watson_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Standard_measurements_and_sexual_dimorphism">Standard measurements and sexual dimorphism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=White-tailed_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Standard measurements and sexual dimorphism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:White-tailed_eagle_LGphoto.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/White-tailed_eagle_LGphoto.jpg/220px-White-tailed_eagle_LGphoto.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="148" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/White-tailed_eagle_LGphoto.jpg/330px-White-tailed_eagle_LGphoto.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/White-tailed_eagle_LGphoto.jpg/440px-White-tailed_eagle_LGphoto.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5366" data-file-height="3618" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:White-tailed_eagle_LGphoto.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Two adult white-tailed eagles in snow in <a href="/wiki/F%C3%A4rnebofj%C3%A4rden_National_Park" title="Färnebofjärden National Park">Färnebofjärden National Park</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nederlandsche_vogelen_(KB)_-_Haliaeetus_albicilla_(418b).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Nederlandsche_vogelen_%28KB%29_-_Haliaeetus_albicilla_%28418b%29.jpg/220px-Nederlandsche_vogelen_%28KB%29_-_Haliaeetus_albicilla_%28418b%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="328" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Nederlandsche_vogelen_%28KB%29_-_Haliaeetus_albicilla_%28418b%29.jpg/330px-Nederlandsche_vogelen_%28KB%29_-_Haliaeetus_albicilla_%28418b%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Nederlandsche_vogelen_%28KB%29_-_Haliaeetus_albicilla_%28418b%29.jpg/440px-Nederlandsche_vogelen_%28KB%29_-_Haliaeetus_albicilla_%28418b%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="1490" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nederlandsche_vogelen_(KB)_-_Haliaeetus_albicilla_(418b).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An illustration of the large bill and sharply curved talons of a juvenile white-tailed eagle.</div></div></div>
<p>The most reliable method to sex birds is by tarsus width and depth and bill depth but these are infrequently measured.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHelanderHailerVilà2007_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHelanderHailerVilà2007-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> <sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> In some cases females are as much as 25% heavier and 15% greater in linear dimensions, though the sexes are rarely this discrepant in standard measurements.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_9-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Among standard measurements, the <a href="/wiki/Wing_chord_(biology)" title="Wing chord (biology)">wing chord</a> is 552 to 695&#160;mm (21.7 to 27.4&#160;in) in males, averaging 606 and 645&#160;mm (23.9 and 25.4&#160;in) in European adults and juveniles, respectively, and 646.5&#160;mm (25.45&#160;in) in Greenland males.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_32-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cramp_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cramp-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> In females, the wing chord may measure 605 to 740&#160;mm (23.8 to 29.1&#160;in), averaging 668 and 685&#160;mm (26.3 and 27.0&#160;in) in European adults and juveniles, respectively, and 691.3&#160;mm (27.22&#160;in) in Greenland females.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_32-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cramp_38-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cramp-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>  Adult <a href="/wiki/Tail" title="Tail">tail</a> length is 250 to 331&#160;mm (9.8 to 13.0&#160;in) in males, averaging 280&#160;mm (11&#160;in), and 276 to 330&#160;mm (10.9 to 13.0&#160;in) in females, averaging 305&#160;mm (12.0&#160;in). Juvenile tail lengths can reach roughly 380&#160;mm (15&#160;in) in both sexes, however.<sup id="cite_ref-Cramp_38-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cramp-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Tarsus_(skeleton)" title="Tarsus (skeleton)">tarsus</a> is 90 to 101&#160;mm (3.5 to 4.0&#160;in), averaging 95.5&#160;mm (3.76&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Zhengjie_37-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zhengjie-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cramp_38-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cramp-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> In terms of their killing apparatuses, their <a href="/wiki/Toe" title="Toe">hallux claw</a>, the largest talon on all accipitrids, is 37 to 46&#160;mm (1.5 to 1.8&#160;in) in length, averaging 40.9&#160;mm (1.61&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Cramp_38-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cramp-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> The hallux claw averages about a centimeter less than that of a <a href="/wiki/Golden_eagle" title="Golden eagle">golden eagle</a> and is more sharply curved, an adaptation to prevent escape of slippery prey such as fish, while that of a bald eagle is similarly about 40.4&#160;mm (1.59&#160;in) and of similar curvature.<sup id="cite_ref-Bortolotti_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bortolotti-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Zylo_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zylo-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Culmen_(bird)" class="mw-redirect" title="Culmen (bird)">exposed culmen</a> is typically large as in all <i>Haliaeetus</i>, ranging from 45 to 65&#160;mm (1.8 to 2.6&#160;in), with an average of 56.1&#160;mm (2.21&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_9-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Zhengjie_37-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zhengjie-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cramp_38-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cramp-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-indianbiodiversity_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-indianbiodiversity-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> The average culmen length of the bald eagle is 54.3&#160;mm (2.14&#160;in), thus averaging slightly smaller. However, the average culmen length in the large bald eagles of Alaska is considerably larger than other bald eagles as well as most white-tailed eagles at up to 75&#160;mm (3.0&#160;in) and can even rival the length (but perhaps not the girth) of the truly massive bill of the Steller's sea eagle.<sup id="cite_ref-Friedmann_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Friedmann-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Imler_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Imler-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Zylo_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zylo-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coloring_and_field_appearance">Coloring and field appearance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=White-tailed_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Coloring and field appearance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3><p>
The adult white-tailed eagle is a greyish mid-brown color overall. The plumage is fairly uniform over most of the body and the wing but the upper wing coverts are typically somewhat paler. Contrasting with the rest of the plumage in the adult are a clearly paler looking head, neck and upper breast which is most often a buffy hue. In worn or bleached plumages these light areas can be even paler at times, ranging to nearly whitish, which can render a resemblance in such eagles to a washed-out <a href="/wiki/Bald_eagle" title="Bald eagle">bald eagle</a>. Some of the palest birds are also washed out looking even on the body which can appear anywhere from cream-tawny to light grayish. It is thought that in some populations perhaps paleness increases with age, although it is possible that there is an historic genetic factor to such pale variations. On the contrary, some adults can also be a richer, more deeply dark brown (or somewhat rufescent) than average with perhaps a mild increase in average darkness of hue to the east of the species’ range.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_9-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHailerHelanderFolkestadGanusevich2007_24-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHailerHelanderFolkestadGanusevich2007-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> When many of the feathers are freshly molted, they can take on a slight purplish gloss.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_32-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> The brownish hue of the adult overall makes the somewhat wedge-shaped white tail stand out in contrast.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_9-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> All the bare parts of their body on adults are yellow in color, including the bill, cere, feet and eyes.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Juvenile and immature are a much darker brown than the adult white-tailed eagle and are more unevenly marked, with whitish feather edgings variably showing, mostly manifesting in some small areas of the underside and under-wing, with a narrow white axillary strip usually apparent. </p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Immature_white-tailed_eagle_plumage.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Immature_white-tailed_eagle_plumage.jpg/220px-Immature_white-tailed_eagle_plumage.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="330" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Immature_white-tailed_eagle_plumage.jpg/330px-Immature_white-tailed_eagle_plumage.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Immature_white-tailed_eagle_plumage.jpg/440px-Immature_white-tailed_eagle_plumage.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1562" data-file-height="2343" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Immature_white-tailed_eagle_plumage.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Plumage of an immature white-tailed eagle</div></div></div><p>  Their upperside is usually similarly darkish brown but variable based on extent of blackish-brown tip to otherwise buff-brown feathers of the mantle, back and upper wing. The head of juveniles is normally a blackish-brown hue, somewhat darker and always more uniform than most other feathers. The juvenile white-tailed eagle's tail tends to be a washed out greyish-cream color with messy blackish color on the feather edges and on the tips. Some individual juveniles may evidence some faint barring on the tail while others may appear largely dirty whitish on the tail. The bill of juveniles is usually nearly half dark brown from the tip and half dirty, dull yellowish or gray to the base, while the feet are usually a dirty yellow and the eyes are a darkish brown. Juvenile males may average a slightly darker brown plumage with less speckling on their upper body than like-age females, their head and neck plumes may also appear shorter, which can accentuate the slighter, more angular skull possessed by males. In disposition, the male juveniles are said to be more highly strung and higher voiced than their female counterparts.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_9-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brown_32-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> The head gradually grows paler over several years. The whitish mottling may increase on the upperparts, belly and especially on the underwing area later into their 3rd year (considered the first subadult plumage) and subadult birds can appear fairly blotched with white but much individual variation in coloring is known at this age. However, this white mottling then fades late into the 4th year and the plumage becomes less contrasting.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_9-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Although sexual maturity is considered to be attained at 5 to 6 years of age, usually the fully white tail and the uniform pale head and neck are not obtained until the 8th year.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_9-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brown_32-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Juveniles first molt in May/June until October/November at just over a year of age. Their 2nd molt is the following year in March or April, with two more subsequent molts usually beginning around this time for the next couple years. Like other large raptors, feathers are molted at slow intervals in order to not inhibit food capture. Only relatively small portions of the flight feathers are molted each year. Molts occur more or less continuously, although it may pause in winter if food is in short supply.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p><div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Haliaeetus_albicilla_(Svolv%C3%A6r,_2012).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Haliaeetus_albicilla_%28Svolv%C3%A6r%2C_2012%29.jpg/220px-Haliaeetus_albicilla_%28Svolv%C3%A6r%2C_2012%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Haliaeetus_albicilla_%28Svolv%C3%A6r%2C_2012%29.jpg/330px-Haliaeetus_albicilla_%28Svolv%C3%A6r%2C_2012%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Haliaeetus_albicilla_%28Svolv%C3%A6r%2C_2012%29.jpg/440px-Haliaeetus_albicilla_%28Svolv%C3%A6r%2C_2012%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3292" data-file-height="2195" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Haliaeetus_albicilla_(Svolv%C3%A6r,_2012).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Adult, wild eagle from <a href="/wiki/Svolv%C3%A6r" title="Svolvær">Svolvær</a>, <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a> showing characteristic long, broad, fingered wings, heavy bill and short wedge-shaped tail</div></div></div>
<p>White-tailed eagles are of all ages typically perch in quite upright positions on exposed branch, rock or other vantage point, but tend to sit more horizontally on the ground or other level surfaces. They have an ample bill with a relatively high culmen, helping impart a relatively narrow and high crowned facial look, especially compared to <a href="/wiki/Aquila_(genus)" title="Aquila (genus)"><i>Aquila</i> eagles</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_9-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Forsman_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forsman-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> Their neck is at times unexceptedly long-looking, more so than the bald eagle, which can give the upper body a vulturine appearance. The tail is relatively short, in some adults it can appear almost strangely so in relation to the massive body, and slightly wedge-shaped. All ages have a well-feathered tibia but bare tarsi. In flight, the white-tailed eagle's wings are extremely broad and deeply fingered (usually at least 6 fingers tend to be visible), creating a "flying door" effect. Juvenile are longer tailed, which is usually more evident in flying than perched birds, with sometimes a slightly bulging section of feathers manifesting on the wing secondaries. The species tends to fly with shallow wing beats, at times their beats can be fairly fast for bird of this size interspersed at times with glides or not gliding at all. At a great distance, they may suggest a huge brown <a href="/wiki/Heron" title="Heron">heron</a> due to this flight style. The wings are held flat or slightly upraised at tip in flight and the species is well known to soar extensively. The white-tailed eagles can be surprisingly maneuverable on the wing, usually during aerial displays or dogfights with other birds. The eagles may too maneuver by half-closing both wings or closing one wing.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_9-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Porter_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Porter-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vocalizations">Vocalizations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=White-tailed_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Vocalizations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The white-tailed eagle is considered a very vocal bird of prey during the breeding season, although some authors consider their voice "not loud or impressive for the size of the bird".<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_9-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brown_32-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> The male call is oft transcribed as <i>gri-gri-gri</i> or <i>krick-krick-krick</i>, while the female is a deeper <i>gra-gra-gra-gra</i> or <i>krau-krau-krau-krau</i>. These will increase in tempo and pitch, with about 15-30 calls in a sequence. Often pairs will duet during early spring, in flight or from a perch.  When perched, the male calls <i>kyi-kyi-kyi-kli-kliek-yak</i> with the head thrown back and upwards in the last call ended with a lower <i>ko-ko-ko</i>, the perched call of females is similar but deeper, a <i>krau-krau-krau-uik-ik</i>. Typically, the perched version of their calls tend to be shriller and higher than those issued in flight.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_9-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brown_32-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> In courtship display, male calls <i>krau-krau-krau-uik-ik-ik</i> answered by females with a lower <i>ra-rack-rack-rack-rack</i>. Young in nest call a shrilly <i>piieh-piieh</i>, while the female when receiving food from male calls out with <i>tschie-tschie</i> or <i>vueee-vueee</i>. Single or repeated <i>krlee</i> or similar component of calls used in other circumstances, but this can be very variable. Alarm calls tend to be 3-4 short, loud <i>klee</i> or <i>klek</i> notes. Sometimes a different call of alarm or anger, a deep <i>gah-gah-gah</i> or <i>jok-jok-jok</i>, similar to alarm calls of a large <a href="/wiki/Gull" title="Gull">gull</a>, is also uttered when a nest is approached (usually recorded while directed towards humans). The young let out a monotonous <i>veee-veee</i> when hungry (or "bored") which intensifies if the eaglets are not fed or brooded immediately.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_9-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brown_32-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mecionis_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mecionis-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Identification">Identification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=White-tailed_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Identification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:White-tailed_Eagle_34.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/White-tailed_Eagle_34.jpg/220px-White-tailed_Eagle_34.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="153" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/White-tailed_Eagle_34.jpg/330px-White-tailed_Eagle_34.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/White-tailed_Eagle_34.jpg/440px-White-tailed_Eagle_34.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="712" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:White-tailed_Eagle_34.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>This is an example of a darker, more richly colored adult than average</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:White_tailed_eagle_raftsund_juvenile.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/White_tailed_eagle_raftsund_juvenile.jpg/220px-White_tailed_eagle_raftsund_juvenile.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="330" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/White_tailed_eagle_raftsund_juvenile.jpg/330px-White_tailed_eagle_raftsund_juvenile.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/White_tailed_eagle_raftsund_juvenile.jpg/440px-White_tailed_eagle_raftsund_juvenile.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2287" data-file-height="3431" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:White_tailed_eagle_raftsund_juvenile.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A typical white tailed eagle juvenile.</div></div></div>
<p>Given reasonable view, adult white-tailed eagles are difficult to mistake for any other bird. There are no other eagles with fully white tails in their range except for in the easternmost limits of their range, their cousins the <a href="/wiki/Bald_eagle" title="Bald eagle">bald</a> and <a href="/wiki/Steller%27s_sea_eagle" title="Steller&#39;s sea eagle">Steller's sea eagles</a>, which in adults are obviously very different in all other respects of plumage.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_9-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Even in poor light, the bald species shows a sharp demarcation from white to dark brown whereas the color contrast is far subtler in white-tailed eagles between their brown body (of a paler hue than that of a bald eagle) and buff-colored head. At a great distance, the adult may be potentially confusable with the <a href="/wiki/Griffon_vulture" title="Griffon vulture">Griffon vulture</a> (<i>Gys fulvus</i>), as the coloring of the two species is vaguely similar and they can overlap somewhat in size although the vulture can average rather heavier and longer winged. However even at long range, the relatively tiny head, distinctly curved trailing wing-edges and more raised wings make the vulture distinctive from the white-tailed eagle.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_9-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Porter_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Porter-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> Juveniles may be harder to distinguish, mainly from other sea eagles in few areas of overlap. In northern <a href="/wiki/Mongolia" title="Mongolia">Mongolia</a> (perhaps spilling over into southern <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a>), the northern part of the <a href="/wiki/Caspian_Sea" title="Caspian Sea">Caspian Sea</a> and some central and southern parts of <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a>, the white-tailed eagle may (or may not) live alongside the rarer, relatively poorly-known <a href="/wiki/Pallas%27s_fish_eagle" title="Pallas&#39;s fish eagle">Pallas's fish eagle</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> The Pallas's juveniles are more distinctively whitish marked on the underwing.  In flight or perched, the Pallas's fish eagle are usually markedly smaller and slighter than white-tailed eagles with a longer and differently marked tail. At all ages, the white-tailed eagle averages a duller, slightly darker and browner colour overall than the Pallas's fish eagle. Pallas's fish eagles are mid-brown on the body in juvenile plumage with no paler feather edging as seen in juveniles and especially subadults of the larger species. Adult Pallas's fish eagles are immediately distinctive rufous hue with a more restricted but more sharply demarked paler buffy head.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_9-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> Bald eagle juveniles may be found together with white-tailed eagles in the <a href="/wiki/Aleutian_islands" class="mw-redirect" title="Aleutian islands">Aleutian islands</a> (where the white-tailed eagle formerly bred until about 30 years ago) and when vagrants of white-tails occur in Alaska. Juveniles of bald and white-tailed eagles often strongly resemble each other but the bald eagles have a shorter neck, a relatively longer and squarer tail, and somewhat less broad wings. In the coloring, the bald juvenile is similarly as dark or even darker brown above as white-tailed eagle juveniles but on the underside often has more extensive whitish mottling, especially on the underwing.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_9-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Howell_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Howell-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> Steller's sea eagles are usually distinctly larger and longer tailed, with a taller, bulkier look in eagles standing on the ground or perched. Steller's juveniles have a different wing shape (roughly paddle-shaped) and a considerably more massive and paler bill, which is yellow even in juveniles unlike in bald and white-tailed eagles. Juvenile Steller's sea eagles are a distinctly darker soot colour than juvenile white-tailed eagles with even less whitish showing on the body than the latter species but, on the other hand, the underwing often as white marked as juvenile bald eagles (dissimilarly from juvenile white-tailed eagles), albeit in different pattern. In all three large northern sea eagles, the tail color is similar at the various stages of development but the shape is more distinctive, especially the bolder wedge shape of the Steller's.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_9-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Howell_57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Howell-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Cinereous_vulture" title="Cinereous vulture">cinereous vulture</a> (<i>Aegypius monachus</i>) may too be considered superficially similar to the juvenile white-tailed eagle, but it is considerably larger and longer-winged and possesses a more uniform and darker hue with conspicuous paler legs and a relatively smaller head. Young white-tailed eagles are also potentially confusable with any <i><a href="/wiki/Aquila_(genus)" title="Aquila (genus)">Aquila</a></i>, but should be obvious even as a silhouette in its huge wings, relatively truncated and slightly wedge-shaped tail and obvious projection of the neck and head. All <i>Aquila</i> lack pale the axillary band often visible on juvenile and subadult white-tailed eagles. Some <a href="/wiki/Greater_spotted_eagle" title="Greater spotted eagle">greater spotted eagles</a> (<i>Clanga clanga</i>) can suggest the wing shape of a white-tailed eagle but are far smaller and shorter winged and never bear a protruding head.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_9-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Forsman_51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forsman-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> Similarly, the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_imperial_eagle" title="Eastern imperial eagle">eastern imperial eagle</a> (<i>Aquila heliaca</i>) may suggest a white-tailed eagle in its flat wing profile and relatively large head and neck but is also visibly smaller with far less broad wings and a relatively longer tail. Like all <i><a href="/wiki/Clanga_(genus)" title="Clanga (genus)">Clanga</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Aquila_(genus)" title="Aquila (genus)">Aquila</a></i>, both greater spotted and eastern imperial eagles should be obviously distinct from white-tailed eagles by plumage characteristics.<sup id="cite_ref-Forsman_51-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forsman-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Clark_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clark-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> The golden eagle usually appears slightly smaller than the white-tailed eagle and tends to be more dashing in flight, which is usually done with a distinct dihedral. When perched, the golden eagle looks more sleek and compact than the rangier white-tailed eagle and tends to be a darker, richer hue of brown. Golden eagles have a much shorter neck, with a smaller head and bill and a longer, squarer tail. White wing patches of juveniles are also differently distributed in golden than juvenile white-tailed eagles.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Distribution_and_habitat">Distribution and habitat</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=White-tailed_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Distribution and habitat">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:White_Tailed_Eagle_AdF.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/White_Tailed_Eagle_AdF.jpg/220px-White_Tailed_Eagle_AdF.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/White_Tailed_Eagle_AdF.jpg/330px-White_Tailed_Eagle_AdF.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/White_Tailed_Eagle_AdF.jpg/440px-White_Tailed_Eagle_AdF.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2464" data-file-height="1632" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:White_Tailed_Eagle_AdF.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Closeup of white-tailed eagle calling</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Haliaeetus_albicilla_-Littleisland,_Norway_-adult-8a.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Haliaeetus_albicilla_-Littleisland%2C_Norway_-adult-8a.jpg/220px-Haliaeetus_albicilla_-Littleisland%2C_Norway_-adult-8a.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="153" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Haliaeetus_albicilla_-Littleisland%2C_Norway_-adult-8a.jpg/330px-Haliaeetus_albicilla_-Littleisland%2C_Norway_-adult-8a.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Haliaeetus_albicilla_-Littleisland%2C_Norway_-adult-8a.jpg/440px-Haliaeetus_albicilla_-Littleisland%2C_Norway_-adult-8a.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="714" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Haliaeetus_albicilla_-Littleisland,_Norway_-adult-8a.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An adult eagle at the <a href="/wiki/Littleisland_lighthouse" class="mw-redirect" title="Littleisland lighthouse">Littleisland lighthouse</a> in Norway</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Haliaeetus_albicilla_-Littleisland,_Norway_-juvenile-8a_(1).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Haliaeetus_albicilla_-Littleisland%2C_Norway_-juvenile-8a_%281%29.jpg/220px-Haliaeetus_albicilla_-Littleisland%2C_Norway_-juvenile-8a_%281%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="164" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Haliaeetus_albicilla_-Littleisland%2C_Norway_-juvenile-8a_%281%29.jpg/330px-Haliaeetus_albicilla_-Littleisland%2C_Norway_-juvenile-8a_%281%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Haliaeetus_albicilla_-Littleisland%2C_Norway_-juvenile-8a_%281%29.jpg/440px-Haliaeetus_albicilla_-Littleisland%2C_Norway_-juvenile-8a_%281%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="765" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Haliaeetus_albicilla_-Littleisland,_Norway_-juvenile-8a_(1).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A young eagle above its nest at <a href="/wiki/Littleisland_lighthouse" class="mw-redirect" title="Littleisland lighthouse">Littleisland lighthouse</a></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Breeding_range">Breeding range</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=White-tailed_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Breeding range">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>This eagle breeds in northern Europe and northern Asia. Their range extends to as far west as southern <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a> (prevented from breeding further north due to the short summers<sup id="cite_ref-DOFNorthGL_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DOFNorthGL-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>), northern and eastern <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a>, and the reintroduced populations in some areas of <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a> (introduced in 2019<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>), <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>, particularly conserved coastal spots. In mainland Europe, often isolated outposts remain in coastal and western <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a> (broadly), northern and southwestern <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Stjernberg_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stjernberg-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> eastern <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a>, southern <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a> (and some spots in the north),<sup id="cite_ref-Ehmsen_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ehmsen-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> islands of the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Sea" title="Baltic Sea">Baltic Sea</a>, western <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austria</a>, northeastern <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>, northern and eastern <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Bogucki_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bogucki-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Czech_Republic" title="Czech Republic">Czech Republic</a>, much of the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_states" title="Baltic states">east Baltic countries</a>, the non-montane areas of the <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a>, eastern <a href="/wiki/Slovenia" title="Slovenia">Slovenia</a>, central and southern <a href="/wiki/Hungary" title="Hungary">Hungary</a> (and adjacent northeastern <a href="/wiki/Croatia" title="Croatia">Croatia</a>), very sporadically in <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Danube_river" class="mw-redirect" title="Danube river">Danube sections</a> of <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bulgaria" title="Bulgaria">Bulgaria</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a><sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> and western and eastern <a href="/wiki/Moldova" title="Moldova">Moldova</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-iucn_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iucn-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_9-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Helander_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Helander-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bijleveld_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bijleveld-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> The bird returned to the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a> in 2006 and in 2018 the number of breeding pairs had increased to 14.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" class="mw-redirect" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a>, it only remains as a breeder in very sparse and small pockets of <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a> and <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgia</a>, taken as a region there is likely to be fewer than 30 breeding pairs in this region.<sup id="cite_ref-iucn_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iucn-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_9-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a>, the white-tailed eagle may still be found breeding only along the southern coast of the <a href="/wiki/Caspian_Sea" title="Caspian Sea">Caspian Sea</a> in northern <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> and southwestern <a href="/wiki/Turkmenistan" title="Turkmenistan">Turkmenistan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-iucn_1-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iucn-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Firouz_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Firouz-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> Discontinuously, they are found as residents in <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a> where they live in a long strip of the southern part of the country starting at the <a href="/wiki/Aral_Sea" title="Aral Sea">Aral Sea</a> and the northwestern portion (but not, as far as is known, breeding in the Kazakh part of the <a href="/wiki/Caspian_Sea" title="Caspian Sea">Caspian Sea</a> coast).<sup id="cite_ref-iucn_1-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iucn-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_9-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> The only country in which the white-tailed eagle is found over a continuous and extremely large area is <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>. The species is found very broadly everywhere in <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> from <a href="/wiki/European_Russia" title="European Russia">European Russia</a> in the west to the <a href="/wiki/Bering_Sea" title="Bering Sea">Bering Sea</a> in the east, only being fully absent as a nester as far as is known from the high Arctic regions and a section bordering westernmost <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a>, although it breeds to south of this in the Russian coastal part of the Caspian Sea.<sup id="cite_ref-iucn_1-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iucn-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_9-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Knystautas_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Knystautas-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Their northern limits occur in Russia to the <a href="/wiki/Ob_river" class="mw-redirect" title="Ob river">Ob river</a> to 70 degrees north at the mouth of the <a href="/wiki/Yenisei_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Yenisei River">Yenisei River</a> and on the <a href="/wiki/Gyda_Peninsula" title="Gyda Peninsula">Gyda</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yamal_Peninsula" title="Yamal Peninsula">Yamal Peninsulas</a>, to the <a href="/wiki/Kolyma_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Kolyma River">Kolyma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indigirka_river" class="mw-redirect" title="Indigirka river">Indigirka</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lena_river" class="mw-redirect" title="Lena river">Lena rivers</a> to above 72 degrees north, even to 75 degrees north on the <a href="/wiki/Taymyr_Peninsula" title="Taymyr Peninsula">Taymyr Peninsula</a>. They are said to be common around the <a href="/wiki/White_Sea" title="White Sea">White Sea</a>, reportedly even the most abundant bird of prey locally and found both on coasts and inland lakes, although breeding rates are low due to the frigid weather.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Knystautas_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Knystautas-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Flerov_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Flerov-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> From Russia, breeding populations spill somewhat into northernmost <a href="/wiki/Mongolia" title="Mongolia">Mongolia</a>, extreme northwestern <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> and northern <a href="/wiki/North_Korea" title="North Korea">North Korea</a>. The white-tailed eagle also breeds on <a href="/wiki/Sakhalin_Island" class="mw-redirect" title="Sakhalin Island">Sakhalin Island</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Kuril_Islands" title="Kuril Islands">Kuril Islands</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hokkaido" title="Hokkaido">Hokkaido</a>, the northernmost island of <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-iucn_1-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iucn-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_9-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wintering_range">Wintering range</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=White-tailed_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Wintering range">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><div id="mwe_player_0" class="PopUpMediaTransform" style="width:220px;" videopayload="&lt;div class=&quot;mediaContainer&quot; style=&quot;width:852px&quot;&gt;&lt;video id=&quot;mwe_player_1&quot; poster=&quot;//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Zittende_en_vliegende_zeearenden_van_verschillende_leeftijden-4961975.webm/852px--Zittende_en_vliegende_zeearenden_van_verschillende_leeftijden-4961975.webm.jpg&quot;
