<p>The wintering range is less well understood for the white-tailed eagle given the extreme reductions and fluctuations of northern breeding populations over the last few centuries, so that the delineation of regular wintering areas versus areas of mere vagrancy is difficult to ascertain. It is known that a small number winter on <a href="/wiki/Moselle_(department)" title="Moselle (department)">Etang de Lindre</a> of <a href="/wiki/Lorraine" title="Lorraine">Lorraine</a>, <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> as well as an area on the border of France to <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a> around <a href="/wiki/Strasbourg" title="Strasbourg">Strasbourg</a>, with vagrants to elsewhere in France, as well as to <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Malta" title="Malta">Malta</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bijleveld_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bijleveld-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Helander_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Helander-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bent_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bent-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> A well-defined wintering population may occur in much of the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>, even with infrequent modern breeding in the northern coastal areas.<sup id="cite_ref-Rijn_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rijn-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> A non-breeding population is known in western <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a> from <a href="/wiki/North_Rhine-Westphalia" title="North Rhine-Westphalia">North Rhine-Westphalia</a> to <a href="/wiki/Bonn" title="Bonn">Bonn</a>, as well as far northern Germany into southwestern <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-iucn_1-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iucn-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Helander_2-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Helander-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Other established wintering areas are known in Europe in west-central <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>, northern <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austria</a>, fairly broadly in southern <a href="/wiki/Slovakia" title="Slovakia">Slovakia</a> and northern <a href="/wiki/Hungary" title="Hungary">Hungary</a> and a few protected pockets of <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Europe" title="Southeast Europe">Southeast Europe</a> apart from the portions in the north and east where they still breed.<sup id="cite_ref-iucn_1-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iucn-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Helander_2-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Helander-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Intermittent forms of vagrancy and migration (most from eagles that breed in or disperse from Russia) are known to occur in several areas of <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a> countries, <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijan" title="Azerbaijan">Azerbaijan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> down to even the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf</a>, albeit seldom is the species to be found commonly or reliably anywhere in these regions. Further east, rare wintering areas are known in a few small, scarce pockets of <a href="/wiki/Turkmenistan" title="Turkmenistan">Turkmenistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uzbekistan" title="Uzbekistan">Uzbekistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tajikistan" title="Tajikistan">Tajikistan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-iucn_1-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iucn-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_9-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Firouz_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Firouz-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> It is a very rare winter visitor to <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, namely the extreme northwest and along the border of <a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepal</a> to <a href="/wiki/Bhutan" title="Bhutan">Bhutan</a> and extreme northern <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> Scattered pockets of wintering birds are known to occur too in central and southern <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, into northeastern <a href="/wiki/Myanmar" title="Myanmar">Myanmar</a>, and more broadly and regularly in much of northeastern <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brazil_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brazil-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> Good numbers winter too in much of <a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">South Korea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a> down to as far as <a href="/wiki/Honshu" title="Honshu">Honshu</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-iucn_1-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iucn-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brazil_77-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brazil-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> White-tailed eagles dispersing from their breeding grounds or natal sites in the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Far_East" title="Russian Far East">Russian Far East</a> are known to occasionally disperse across the <a href="/wiki/Bering_Sea" title="Bering Sea">Bering Sea</a> to North America in several parts of the <a href="/wiki/Aleutian_Islands" title="Aleutian Islands">Aleutian Islands</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Pribilof_Islands" title="Pribilof Islands">Pribilof Islands</a> and some of mainland coastal <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a> down to <a href="/wiki/Kodiak_Island" title="Kodiak Island">Kodiak Island</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Howell_57-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Howell-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> Some white-tailed eagles even bred in <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a> on <a href="/wiki/Attu_Island" title="Attu Island">Attu Island</a> in the late 1970s to the early 1980s (until 1984 when the last attempts were recorded) but it was not clear whether young were ever successfully fledged.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Habitat">Habitat</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=White-tailed_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Habitat">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Noor_merikotkas_puul..jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Noor_merikotkas_puul..jpg/220px-Noor_merikotkas_puul..jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Noor_merikotkas_puul..jpg/330px-Noor_merikotkas_puul..jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Noor_merikotkas_puul..jpg/440px-Noor_merikotkas_puul..jpg 2x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="1333" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Noor_merikotkas_puul..jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>White-tailed eagles are fairly associated with wooded areas but usually ones quite near water.</div></div></div>
<p>White-tailed eagles may be found in varied habitat but usually are closely associated with <a href="/wiki/Wetland" title="Wetland">water</a> and generally occurs in <a href="/wiki/Upland_and_lowland" title="Upland and lowland">lowland areas</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_9-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Although mainly a lowland species, the species is known to live at elevations of 1,500 to 2,300&#160;m (4,900 to 7,500&#160;ft) so long as there is water access in some parts of <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_32-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> In coastal areas, the species may range from high sea <a href="/wiki/Cliff" title="Cliff">cliffs</a> down to low-lying <a href="/wiki/Island" title="Island">islands</a> and <a href="/wiki/Archipelago" title="Archipelago">archipelagos</a>. Especially in winter, many white-tailed eagles often frequent low coastal spots, <a href="/wiki/Estuary" title="Estuary">estuaries</a> and <a href="/wiki/Saltmarsh" class="mw-redirect" title="Saltmarsh">coastal marshes</a>. Several studies have supported that coastal areas are preferred when available during winter.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_9-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Helander_2-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Helander-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Treinys_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Treinys-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> In many areas, white-tailed eagles can seem to switch freely between usually cliff habitat and <a href="/wiki/Woodland" title="Woodland">wooded spots</a> for nesting sites and the center of their home range habitat.<sup id="cite_ref-Evans_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Evans-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> In some areas, such as <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>, this species may occur in regions with intensive human <a href="/wiki/Fishing" title="Fishing">fishing</a> activity and they may become unusually partially habituated to this human presence.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_9-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Tingay_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tingay-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> Inland, white-tailed eagles usually require secluded woods, forested areas or groups of trees with tall <a href="/wiki/Old_growth_forest" class="mw-redirect" title="Old growth forest">mature trees</a> and access to freshwater <a href="/wiki/Wetland" title="Wetland">wetlands</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Lake" title="Lake">lakes</a>, <a href="/wiki/River" title="River">river systems</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marsh" title="Marsh">marshes</a> or extensive, <a href="/wiki/Agricultural_land" title="Agricultural land">low-disturbance farmland</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_9-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Alluvium" title="Alluvium">alluvial wetlands</a> of <a href="/wiki/Croatia" title="Croatia">Croatia</a>, 95% of nests were found within 4&#160;km (2.5&#160;mi) of deep freshwater.<sup id="cite_ref-Radović_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Radović-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> In some areas, white-tailed eagles readily visit <a href="/wiki/Commercial_fishing" title="Commercial fishing">commercial fish farms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Koi_pond" title="Koi pond">carp ponds</a> and similar areas with easily accessible food but they will usually avoid areas where human disturbances (especially noisy varieties such as <a href="/wiki/Construction" title="Construction">construction</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_water_sports" title="List of water sports">water sporting</a> and heavy <a href="/wiki/Boating" title="Boating">boating</a> activities and <a href="/wiki/Hunting" title="Hunting">hunting</a>) commonly occur.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_9-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Helander_2-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Helander-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> However, forestry activity and resulting lessened numbers of tall mature trees and large tree stands in <a href="/wiki/Estonia" title="Estonia">Estonia</a> was found to affect breeding white-tailed eagles less so than it seemed to affect breeding <a href="/wiki/Black_stork" title="Black stork">black storks</a> (<i>Ciconia nigra</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Rosenvald_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rosenvald-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> On the other hand, from studying wintering white-tailed eagles in partially or heavily disturbed wetlands in parts of the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a> shows that such areas cannot support the eagles for any long-term period and may only be visited for a day or two by individual eagles.<sup id="cite_ref-Rijn_73-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rijn-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Behaviour">Behaviour</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=White-tailed_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Behaviour">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><div id="mwe_player_2" class="PopUpMediaTransform" style="width:220px;" videopayload="&lt;div class=&quot;mediaContainer&quot; style=&quot;width:854px&quot;&gt;&lt;video id=&quot;mwe_player_3&quot; data-height=&quot;480&quot; data-bandwidth=&quot;1231112&quot; data-framerate=&quot;25&quot;/&gt;&lt;source src=&quot;//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/5/50/NuukSeaEagle.ogv/NuukSeaEagle.ogv.720p.vp9.webm&quot; data-height=&quot;360&quot; data-bandwidth=&quot;630648&quot; data-framerate=&quot;25&quot;/&gt;&lt;/video&gt;&lt;/div&gt;"><img alt="File:NuukSeaEagle.ogv" style="width:220px;height:124px" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/NuukSeaEagle.ogv/220px--NuukSeaEagle.ogv.jpg" /><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/50/NuukSeaEagle.ogv" title="Play media" target="new"><span class="play-btn-large"><span class="mw-tmh-playtext">Play media</span></span></a></div>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:NuukSeaEagle.ogv" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Greenland eagle</div></div></div>
<p>White-tailed eagles spend much of their day perched on trees or crags, and may often not move for hours. Perhaps up to 90% of a day may be spent perched, especially if weather is poor.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brown_32-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Also, they will alternate periods of soaring with perching, especially flying over water or well-watered areas, but do considerably less soaring on average than do golden eagles. Pairs regularly roost together, often near to their nest, either on a crag or tree or crevices, overhung ledges or small isolated trees on a crag.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_32-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Migration_and_dispersal">Migration and dispersal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=White-tailed_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Migration and dispersal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The white-tailed eagle may be considered a rather inconsistent and partial migrant.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> The species seldom migrates in the western part of its range, with eagles even breeding as far north as <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a> and coastal <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a> not moving at all for winter, but for some southward juvenile movements following dispersal. Juveniles overall are more migratory and dispersive and leave natal areas sooner, which is by August–September in northwestern Europe and return later, by March/April, than adults do.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_9-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brown_32-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Few Norwegian juveniles, per banding studies, were recorded to travel any considerable distance. Extreme cases include one that was found 720&#160;km (450&#160;mi) south of its nest near <a href="/wiki/Karlstad" title="Karlstad">Karlstad</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a>, another set of 4 color marked 1st year juveniles were also recorded to turn up in southern <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a> but one was found down to the <a href="/wiki/Wadden_Sea" title="Wadden Sea">Wadden Sea</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a> and it is likely that trickling down numbers of Norwegian post-dispersal juveniles have gone onto form much of the known Dutch white-tailed eagle population.<sup id="cite_ref-Rijn_73-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rijn-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hagen,Y._1976_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hagen,Y._1976-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> In contrast, young from <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a> tend to distribute to the southwesterly direction to the shores of the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_sea" class="mw-redirect" title="Baltic sea">Baltic sea</a>. One from Finland was recovered 520&#160;km (320&#160;mi) west in northern Norway and another was found as far south as <a href="/wiki/Bulgaria" title="Bulgaria">Bulgaria</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Stjernberg_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stjernberg-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> In more southerly areas, winter movements are drawn-out and irregular, with most mature pairs probably never leaving their nesting haunts year-around.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_32-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Those that breed on inland waterways may migrate to sea coast.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_32-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> German juveniles usually do not travel far, with most recorded to travel less than 50&#160;km (31&#160;mi) from their nests and a majority staying near the Baltic coast. However, some eagles that hatched from nests in Germany have been found as far south as in <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>, 1,030&#160;km (640&#160;mi) away to the southeast, or to <a href="/wiki/Gironde" title="Gironde">Gironde</a>, <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>, 1,520&#160;km (940&#160;mi) to the southwest.<sup id="cite_ref-vonBlotzheim_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vonBlotzheim-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> In several parts of <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, quite unlike many European populations, the white-tailed eagle seems to be largely migratory.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_9-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> In the far east, the species appear to take different migratory routes in fall and spring, traveling from north-central <a href="/wiki/Kamchatka" class="mw-redirect" title="Kamchatka">Kamchatka</a> thru the <a href="/wiki/Kurile_islands" class="mw-redirect" title="Kurile islands">Kurile islands</a> to <a href="/wiki/Hokkaido" title="Hokkaido">Hokkaido</a> in fall, while in spring these eagles travel north through <a href="/wiki/Sakhalin" title="Sakhalin">Sakhalin</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Okhotsk_Coast" title="Okhotsk Coast">Okhotsk Coast</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/White_Sea" title="White Sea">White Sea</a> area, southward moments begin in September with most white-tailed eagles being gone by November but in mild winters some adults may remain behind. Some white-tailed eagles from the White Sea were found well over 2,000&#160;km (1,200&#160;mi) away to the west, in countries such as <a href="/wiki/Hungary" title="Hungary">Hungary</a> and <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>. Return spring migration to the White Sea is by February–March.<sup id="cite_ref-Flerov_71-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Flerov-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> During winter, whether long-distance migrants or short-distance dispersers, white-tailed eagles tend to become gregarious, especially younger juvenile birds. Many such groups can contain up to 10 and, in areas near large breeding populations such as in Norway, at least 30-40 individuals.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Willgohs_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willgohs-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Wintering congregations at the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_sea" class="mw-redirect" title="Baltic sea">Baltic coast</a> and on the <a href="/wiki/Elbe" title="Elbe">River Elbe</a> from 37 winters show that arrivals begin in November, with numbers peaking in January and then declining during March and early April.<sup id="cite_ref-vonBlotzheim_88-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vonBlotzheim-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> Although juveniles usually return to their natal area some apparently overshoot these areas, such as those returning to <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a> or on the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a> which have been recorded 330&#160;km (210&#160;mi) north of their natal site and 510&#160;km (320&#160;mi) northeast of their natal site.<sup id="cite_ref-vonBlotzheim_88-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vonBlotzheim-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Territoriality">Territoriality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=White-tailed_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Territoriality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Territory size in white-tailed eagles may vary from 52 to 415&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (20 to 160&#160;sq&#160;mi), usually less than 130&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (50&#160;sq&#160;mi), per one estimate.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_32-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> However, home ranges in northeastern Germany were much smaller than this, at 2.25 to 19.16&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (0.87 to 7.40&#160;sq&#160;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> While territorial behavior is known in well more than half of all modern birds, essentially all predatory birds of different lineages are particularly strongly territorial because the live prey necessary to feed a family, including the female of the pair (which must remain near the young for them to survive) and the young themselves, tends to be sparser. Furthermore, appropriate habitat is needed in which to execute this hunting and also, of course, to build a nest with some security.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> While a relatively gregarious raptor, especially among wintering birds and juveniles and immature birds, they are territorial and intrusion by a male in adult plumage often provokes vigorously fighting, in which either combatant can even die. In some cases, these viscous fights can even cause damage to the nest as the two fighting eagles plummet down trying to slash at each other.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brown_32-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Dietary_biology">Dietary biology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=White-tailed_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Dietary biology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Haliaeetus_albicilla,_Mull_2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Haliaeetus_albicilla%2C_Mull_2.jpg/220px-Haliaeetus_albicilla%2C_Mull_2.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="176" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Haliaeetus_albicilla%2C_Mull_2.jpg/330px-Haliaeetus_albicilla%2C_Mull_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Haliaeetus_albicilla%2C_Mull_2.jpg/440px-Haliaeetus_albicilla%2C_Mull_2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1500" data-file-height="1200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Haliaeetus_albicilla,_Mull_2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>About to grasp a fish near the <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Mull" title="Isle of Mull">Isle of Mull</a>, Scotland</div></div></div>
<p>The white-tailed eagle's diet is varied, opportunistic and seasonal. Prey specimens can often include <a href="/wiki/Fish" title="Fish">fish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bird" title="Bird">birds</a> and, mostly in a secondary capacity, <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_9-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> White-tailed eagles are powerful predators and capable of attacking large prey of considerable sizes but, like most predators, prefer prey that is vulnerable and easy for them to capture.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Valero_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Valero-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> Especially during the winter (and opportunistically in all seasons), many birds of the species live largely as scavengers, usually by coming across available carrion or watching for the activity of <a href="/wiki/Corvid" class="mw-redirect" title="Corvid">corvids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Old_World_vulture" title="Old World vulture">vultures</a> or other raptors.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> White-tailed eagles in northeastern <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a> were shown to hunt mostly from perches, in a “sit-and-wait” style, usually from a prominent tree perch. Like other sea eagles, they can only capture fish normally in the <a href="/wiki/Littoral_zone" title="Littoral zone">littoral zone</a>, seldom hunting fish when they exceed a water depth of 1.5 to 2&#160;m (4.9 to 6.6&#160;ft).<sup id="cite_ref-Valero_92-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Valero-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Nadjafzadeh2_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nadjafzadeh2-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> In addition to trees, they may also use <a href="/wiki/Cliff" title="Cliff">crags</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hillock" title="Hillock">hillocks</a> or high <a href="/wiki/Tussock_(grass)" title="Tussock (grass)">grassy tussocks</a> as hunting perches so long as the perch provides a good overall view of the environment.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Willgohs_31-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willgohs-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Fish tend to be grabbed in a shallow dive after a short distance flight from a perch, usually with the eagles only getting their feet wet.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_9-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Occasionally, though, white-tailed eagles have been recorded <a href="/wiki/Diving_bird" title="Diving bird">plunging</a> right into water, usually while hunting on the wing at a height of at least 200&#160;m (660&#160;ft). In <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a>, plunge-diving is considered rare.<sup id="cite_ref-Willgohs_31-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willgohs-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-vonBlotzheim_88-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vonBlotzheim-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> At times they will also fish by wading into shallows, often from shores or gravel islands.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> The species will at times variously follow <a href="/wiki/Fishing_vessel" title="Fishing vessel">fishing boats</a>, readily exploits commercial <a href="/wiki/Fishery" title="Fishery">fisheries</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fish_stocking" title="Fish stocking">stocked lakes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Koi_pond" title="Koi pond">carp ponds</a> and the like, and scavenges dead fish or <a href="/wiki/Offal" title="Offal">fish-offal</a> in a wide range of situations.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_9-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> When it comes to non-fish prey, it has been said that white-tailed eagles often hunt by flying low over <a href="/wiki/Coast" title="Coast">sea coast</a> or <a href="/wiki/Marsh" title="Marsh">lakeshore</a> and attempt to surprise victims.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_32-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> However, the hunting success rates on healthy birds can be low as revealed in studying wintering eagles in <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a> attempting to hunt <a href="/wiki/Mallard" title="Mallard">mallards</a> (<i>Anas platyrhynchos</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Rudebeck_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rudebeck-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> White-tailed eagles also regularly <a href="/wiki/Kleptoparasitism" title="Kleptoparasitism">pirate</a> food from <a href="/wiki/Otter" title="Otter">otters</a> and other birds including <a href="/wiki/Cormorant" title="Cormorant">cormorants</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gull" title="Gull">gulls</a>, <a href="/wiki/Osprey" title="Osprey">ospreys</a>, <a href="/wiki/Corvid" class="mw-redirect" title="Corvid">corvids</a> and various other <a href="/wiki/Bird_of_prey" title="Bird of prey">raptors</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-vonBlotzheim_88-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vonBlotzheim-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Carrion" title="Carrion">Carrion</a> is often the primary food source during lean winter months, with fish and <a href="/wiki/Ungulate" title="Ungulate">ungulates</a> preferred but everything from <a href="/wiki/Cetacean" class="mw-redirect" title="Cetacean">cetaceans</a> to <a href="/wiki/Livestock" title="Livestock">livestock</a> to even <a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">humans</a> being eaten after death.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_9-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-rspb_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rspb-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> From studies of captive white-tailed eagles, daily food requirements were estimated at 500 to 600&#160;g (1.1 to 1.3&#160;lb), which is equivalent to about 10% of the birds' body weight, with crop contents commonly of 190–560&#160;g (0.42–1.23&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_32-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Uttendorfer_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Uttendorfer-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> Some semi-captive juveniles on the isle of <a href="/wiki/R%C3%B9m" title="Rùm">Rùm</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a> could eat up to 1.4&#160;kg (3.1&#160;lb) in one sitting.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Love2_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love2-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>  However, in Norway, it was estimated that a family of wild white-tailed eagles including each adult and 3 fledglings were consuming on average up to 625&#160;g (1.378&#160;lb) per bird each day. Furthermore, one male consumed an estimated 2&#160;kg (4.4&#160;lb) in a single meal upon capturing a large fish. The crop can bulge to the size of a small <a href="/wiki/Grapefruit" title="Grapefruit">grapefruit</a> after they've consumed a large meal.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Willgohs_31-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willgohs-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Haliaeetus_albicilla_1_(Bohu%C5%A1_%C4%8C%C3%AD%C4%8Del).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Haliaeetus_albicilla_1_%28Bohu%C5%A1_%C4%8C%C3%AD%C4%8Del%29.jpg/220px-Haliaeetus_albicilla_1_%28Bohu%C5%A1_%C4%8C%C3%AD%C4%8Del%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Haliaeetus_albicilla_1_%28Bohu%C5%A1_%C4%8C%C3%AD%C4%8Del%29.jpg/330px-Haliaeetus_albicilla_1_%28Bohu%C5%A1_%C4%8C%C3%AD%C4%8Del%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Haliaeetus_albicilla_1_%28Bohu%C5%A1_%C4%8C%C3%AD%C4%8Del%29.jpg/440px-Haliaeetus_albicilla_1_%28Bohu%C5%A1_%C4%8C%C3%AD%C4%8Del%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="682" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Haliaeetus_albicilla_1_(Bohu%C5%A1_%C4%8C%C3%AD%C4%8Del).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In winter, white-tailed eagles often live largely on carrion.</div></div></div>
<p>Many studies have reflected that the primary foods of white-tailed eagles are <a href="/wiki/Fish" title="Fish">fish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Water_bird" title="Water bird">water birds</a>. These are the primary food as well for other sea eagle species. However, unlike most <i>Haliaeetus</i>, including the <a href="/wiki/Bald_eagle" title="Bald eagle">bald eagle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Steller%27s_sea_eagle" title="Steller&#39;s sea eagle">Steller's sea eagle</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Water_bird" title="Water bird">water birds</a> tend to take the primary position in the diet.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_9-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mlíkovský_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mlíkovský-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> From 26 accumulated food studies for this species, prey remains and pellets show that about 48.5% of the diet is made up of <a href="/wiki/Bird" title="Bird">birds</a>, 39.95% by <a href="/wiki/Fish" title="Fish">fish</a>, 9.95% by <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a> and 1.6% by other foods. In total, more than 300 prey species are known throughout the bird's range.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Vrezec_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vrezec-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> However, based on studies of prey remains and pellets in laboratories from <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a> white-tailed eagles, birds were shown to be biased in both kinds of remains (pellets and prey remains) whereas in situ study and direct nest observation favor fish. Going on pellet/remains alone here in Greenland from 557 items in the 1979 study, 68% of the diet would’ve been represented by birds and only 20% by fish but comprehensive observation shifted it to show fish were the primary food at 58% and birds were secondary at 30%. This study claimed this is often because the bones of fish are dissolved by the large digestive tract of the eagles upon consumption and may thus leave almost nothing in remains and to some extent in pellets. Subsequent studies here showed a much stronger preference for fish in Greenland by 1983, as fish comprised an extreme 91.8% of 660 items.<sup id="cite_ref-Wille_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wille-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kampp_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kampp-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> However, this kind of direct continuous observation of food deliveries to nests is not always possible.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Furthermore, despite similar bias for prey that is large and leaves conspicuous remains (including any larger fish, bird or mammal), in the bald eagle it was found that fish were usually detectable and dominant in remains and pellets. Most modern biologists may need to leave some fish unidentified but will account for different methodologies of prey study to get the most complete picture attainable.<sup id="cite_ref-Valero_92-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Valero-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> During winter, mammal prey can become more important in foods locally, as indicated in Scotland and shown in Norway and eastern Germany.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> As much as 41% of the diet can be made up of mammals, as was the case on the <a href="/wiki/Kola_Peninsula" title="Kola Peninsula">Kola Peninsula</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Flerov_71-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Flerov-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> There is evidence of strong seasonal shifts in food habits in several parts of the range, usually the largest portions of fish are caught during warmer months while birds and mammals are more important in the colder months, especially in coastal areas such as <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a> when preferred fish prey often move to deeper water during winter.<sup id="cite_ref-Willgohs_31-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willgohs-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Dornbusch_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dornbusch-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> Among both <a href="/wiki/Fish" title="Fish">fish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bird" title="Bird">bird</a> prey, it is thought that a majority that are caught weigh between 0.5 and 3&#160;kg (1.1 and 6.6&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> At times it has been said that “most” prey of white-tailed eagles will weigh only 0.5 to 1&#160;kg (1.1 to 2.2&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-Sulkava_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sulkava-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> However, the mean prey sizes caught can show greater variability. Three studies showed that mean prey size varied from 578&#160;g (1.274&#160;lb) in the <a href="/wiki/Wigry_National_Park" title="Wigry National Park">Wigry National Park</a>, <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a>,1,062.1&#160;g (2.342&#160;lb) in the <a href="/wiki/Rybinsk_Reservoir" title="Rybinsk Reservoir">Rybinsk Reservoir</a>, <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> and 1.72&#160;kg (3.8&#160;lb) in the <a href="/wiki/Volga-Kama_Nature_Reserve" title="Volga-Kama Nature Reserve">Volga-Kama Nature Reserve</a>, <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Zawadzka_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zawadzka-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> Thus, the mean prey size falls just slightly short of the mean prey mass of the golden eagle, which globally averages about 1.35 to 1.63&#160;kg (3.0 to 3.6&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fish">Fish</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=White-tailed_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Fish">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sea_Eagle_(14992790955).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Sea_Eagle_%2814992790955%29.jpg/220px-Sea_Eagle_%2814992790955%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="142" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Sea_Eagle_%2814992790955%29.jpg/330px-Sea_Eagle_%2814992790955%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Sea_Eagle_%2814992790955%29.jpg/440px-Sea_Eagle_%2814992790955%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3823" data-file-height="2460" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sea_Eagle_(14992790955).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>White-tailed eagle with caught fish.</div></div></div>
<p>Overall, nearly 70 species of fish are known to be taken from throughout the white-tailed eagle's range.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Vrezec_98-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vrezec-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> White-tailed eagles can hunt fish in fresh or saltwater as well as those that prefer brackish water areas. However, they are basically restricted to taking fish in extremely shallow water, often by preference in water less than 1&#160;m (3.3&#160;ft) deep.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Ideal fishing areas can be found in areas such as the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_sea" class="mw-redirect" title="Baltic sea">Baltic sea</a>, where low coasts and archipelagos often have relatively shallow water.<sup id="cite_ref-Bergman_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bergman-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> While healthy, large fish are often taken as well, white-tailed eagles often take out sickly, injured or already dead fish. In some cases, the fish prey will float to the surface when infected by <a href="/wiki/Diphyllobothrium" title="Diphyllobothrium">fish tapeworm</a>, as is often the case with some fish families such as <a href="/wiki/Carp" title="Carp">carp</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> Fish are also caught after being battered, injured and killed at power plants, from large-scale fishing nets or are taken directly from human fishermen.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Demersal_fish" title="Demersal fish">Benthic fish</a> tend to cling to rocks or sandy soil in shallows may be more vulnerable since they tend to look downward rather upward and are less competent at escaping predators coming from above the water's surface. Therefore, lurking benthic fish such as <a href="/wiki/Lumpsucker" class="mw-redirect" title="Lumpsucker">lumpsuckers</a> are more vulnerable than many.<sup id="cite_ref-Willgohs_31-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willgohs-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Besides vulnerability, habitat and prey behaviour, fish body size may be a driver in the piscivorian dietary preferences. Studies have indicated that fish less than 20&#160;cm (7.9&#160;in) are taken infrequently, since they have a lower yield, fish of up to 30&#160;cm (12&#160;in) are taken secondarily and fish between 30 and 60&#160;cm (0.98 and 1.97&#160;ft) are preferred since they have the highest nutritional benefit. Fish taken can exceed 0.9 to 1&#160;m (3.0 to 3.3&#160;ft) but since they can start to considerably exceed the weight of the eagle itself, they may be prove too difficult to overpower.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Valero_92-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Valero-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Nadjafzadeh2_93-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nadjafzadeh2-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> One large <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_halibut" title="Atlantic halibut">Atlantic halibut</a> (<i>Hippoglossus hippoglossus</i>) was found with the disembodied feet of a white-tailed eagle still imbedded in its back, presumed the eagle drowned after being overpowered and drug under only to rot off, leaving only feet.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> Since they do not have the waterproofing oils on the plumage of the more accomplished raptorial diver, the <a href="/wiki/Osprey" title="Osprey">osprey</a> (<i>Pandion haliaetus</i>), white-tailed eagles prefer not to get their feathers wet as it can take a long time for them to dry. This may also make them vulnerable to losing their catch to other white-tailed eagles since their flight may be impaired until the wings are dried. Therefore, when hunting fish, they will almost always take flight as soon as possible to a feeding perch or nest.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Nadjafzadeh2_93-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nadjafzadeh2-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> The largest fish lifted in flight and flown off with have been claimed anecdotely to reach weights of as much as 15&#160;kg (33&#160;lb) in the case of an <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_halibut" title="Atlantic halibut">Atlantic halibut</a> in <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Willgohs_31-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willgohs-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> However, this is likely reported in error as no living eagle would likely be able to take flight with this heavy a load as they are more or less constrained to carrying in flight a load more or less equal to their own body weight, and even then only laboriously and in favorable wind conditions.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Palmer_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Palmer-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> The same Norwegian study coincided that another eagle when trying to fly with an <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_cod" title="Atlantic cod">Atlantic cod</a> (<i>Gadus morhua</i>) weighing about 8&#160;kg (18&#160;lb), the fish was quickly dropped as it was apparently deemed too heavy.<sup id="cite_ref-Willgohs_31-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willgohs-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> The widely reported case of a white-tailed eagle carrying off a 3.5-year-old girl in Norway flight by the back of her dress only to drop her mostly unharmed below the eagle's eyrie, which was included in the <a href="/wiki/Guinness_Book_of_World_Records" class="mw-redirect" title="Guinness Book of World Records">Guinness Book of World Records</a>, is considered most likely to be apocryphal since she weighed approximately 18&#160;kg (40&#160;lb), far too heavy for any living eagle to lift in flight.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Willgohs_31-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willgohs-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wood_30-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wood-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> Another option when taking particularly large fish is for the eagle to stay in water and row, swimming using their wings, across the water to the nearest bank or shore.  While this would leave them waterlogged, of course, the food yield from such a catch is obviously attractive. White-tailed eagles have been photographed doing this with a large fish successfully in <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a> and 35 such cases were reported in <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a> alone.<sup id="cite_ref-Willgohs_31-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willgohs-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The most frequently recorded prey species in 18 food studies from across the range is the <a href="/wiki/Northern_pike" title="Northern pike">northern pike</a> (<i>Esox lucius</i>), present in at least 16 of those studies. Pike were found to be the main prey species in both the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Sea" title="Baltic Sea">Baltic Sea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lapland_(Sweden)" title="Lapland (Sweden)">Lapland</a> in <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a>, at three breeding locations in <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a>, in two studies from <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a> and in <a href="/wiki/Belarus" title="Belarus">Belarus</a>. The maximum representation of pike known was in <a href="/wiki/Lapland_(Sweden)" title="Lapland (Sweden)">Lapland, Sweden</a> where they comprised 38.2% of 809 food items.<sup id="cite_ref-Vrezec_98-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vrezec-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sulkava_103-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sulkava-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Fischer1_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fischer1-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ekblad_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ekblad-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> Considered a top predator in many freshwater ecosystems (and thus with less significant anti-predator camouflage), the pike may be more conspicuous than many fish to hunting eagles due to the fish's boldly speckled flanks.<sup id="cite_ref-Sulkava_103-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sulkava-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> While an average mature weight for a pike is around 1.4&#160;kg (3.1&#160;lb), white-tailed eagles often attack larger sized pike, with an estimated average weight range of 2 to 5&#160;kg (4.4 to 11.0&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Flerov_71-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Flerov-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> Some pike taken by white-tailed eagles were even estimated to weigh around 12&#160;kg (26&#160;lb), however the eagle would be unable to fly with such a large catch and would need to row it to the nearest shore.<sup id="cite_ref-Adrianova_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Adrianova-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> The next most widely reported fish prey species is the <a href="/wiki/Common_bream" title="Common bream">common bream</a> (<i>Abramis brama</i>). This bream was reported in 10 of 18 dietary studies and was the main prey in the <a href="/wiki/Polesie_State_Radioecological_Reserve" title="Polesie State Radioecological Reserve">Polesie State Radioecological Reserve</a>, <a href="/wiki/Belarus" title="Belarus">Belarus</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Ural_mountains" class="mw-redirect" title="Ural mountains">Ural mountains</a> region of <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, and in the <a href="/wiki/Kostomuksha_Nature_Reserve" title="Kostomuksha Nature Reserve">Kostomuksha Nature Reserve</a>, <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Adrianova_118-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Adrianova-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Yurko_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yurko-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Samago_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Samago-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> Like the pike, the relatively large size of common bream, which weigh about 1.09&#160;kg (2.4&#160;lb) on average, can prove attractive to hunting eagles. They are also vulnerable to tapeworms so can be easily taken while floating close the water's surface in ill condition.<sup id="cite_ref-Valero_92-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Valero-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Adrianova_118-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Adrianova-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Many varieties of fish are taken opportunistically and randomly, as opposed to pike and bream, which can locally appear to be selected out of proportion to their regional population.<sup id="cite_ref-Valero_92-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Valero-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Nadjafzadeh2_93-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nadjafzadeh2-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> Particularly diverse in the white-tailed eagle's prey spectrum are the family <a href="/wiki/Cyprinidae" title="Cyprinidae">Cyprinidae</a>, of which more than 20 species are known to be predated including the common bream.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Vrezec_98-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vrezec-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> Others taken with some preference may include <a href="/wiki/Salmonid" class="mw-redirect" title="Salmonid">salmonids</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gadidae" title="Gadidae">cod and their allies</a>, both families known to obtain relatively large body sizes and occasional habituate shallow water, as well as <a href="/wiki/Lumpsucker" class="mw-redirect" title="Lumpsucker">lumpsuckers</a>, because they are benthic.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a>, the leading prey species is the salmonid, the <a href="/wiki/Arctic_char" title="Arctic char">Arctic char</a> (<i>Salvelinus alpinus</i>), which comprised 27.2% of 660 prey items. The secondary prey in Greenland was recorded as two species of <a href="/wiki/Cod" title="Cod">cod</a>. While the Arctic char can average up to 2.53&#160;kg (5.6&#160;lb) and cod can sometimes grow considerably larger than that, the average weight of cod taken here was estimated at only 420 to 640&#160;g (0.93 to 1.41&#160;lb) and the average char at 660 to 740&#160;g (1.46 to 1.63&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-Wille_99-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wille-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a>, of 524 fish prey items, the <a href="/wiki/Cyclopterus_lumpus" title="Cyclopterus lumpus">common lumpsucker</a> (<i>Cyclopterus lumpus</i>), which averages up to 1.43&#160;kg (3.2&#160;lb) but is usually smaller, made up 24% of fish taken and the 2.13&#160;kg (4.7&#160;lb) <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_wolffish" title="Atlantic wolffish">Atlantic wolffish</a> (<i>Anarhichas lupus</i>) made up 17% of fish taken.<sup id="cite_ref-Willgohs_31-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willgohs-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> However, fish were secondary to birds overall in <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Willgohs_31-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willgohs-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Per two studies from <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a>, fish were usually the main food unlike in Norway and <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a> and could comprise from 51-60% of the diet.<sup id="cite_ref-Vrezec_98-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vrezec-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sulkava_103-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sulkava-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ekblad_115-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ekblad-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> Fish were also somewhat dominant in the foods from two studies in <a href="/wiki/Belarus" title="Belarus">Belarus</a>, making up 48.1-53.7% of the diet.<sup id="cite_ref-Yurko_119-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yurko-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ivanovsky_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ivanovsky-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> Fish similarly were important to nesting eagles in <a href="/wiki/Hokkaido" title="Hokkaido">Hokkaido</a>, where 54% of 533 prey items were fish, led by the 800&#160;g (1.8&#160;lb) <a href="/wiki/Alaska_pollock" title="Alaska pollock">Alaska pollock</a> (<i>Gadus chalcogrammus</i>) at 18.4%.<sup id="cite_ref-Mori_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mori-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> In different studies of the <a href="/wiki/Danube_Delta" title="Danube Delta">Danube Delta</a> of <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a>, from 44.6% to 79% of the diet was comprised by fish, led by the <a href="/wiki/Common_carp" title="Common carp">common carp</a> (<i>Cyprinus carpio</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Prussian_carp" title="Prussian carp">Prussian carp</a> (<i>Carassius gibelio</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sandor_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sandor-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Kostomuksha_Nature_Reserve" title="Kostomuksha Nature Reserve">Kostomuksha Nature Reserve</a> of <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, fish were strongly dominant in food remains, making 80% of the known diet.<sup id="cite_ref-Adrianova_118-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Adrianova-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Birds">Birds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=White-tailed_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Birds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:An_adult_white-tailed_eagle_(Haliaeetus_albicilla)_defending_its_prey.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/An_adult_white-tailed_eagle_%28Haliaeetus_albicilla%29_defending_its_prey.jpg/220px-An_adult_white-tailed_eagle_%28Haliaeetus_albicilla%29_defending_its_prey.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="124" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/An_adult_white-tailed_eagle_%28Haliaeetus_albicilla%29_defending_its_prey.jpg/330px-An_adult_white-tailed_eagle_%28Haliaeetus_albicilla%29_defending_its_prey.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/An_adult_white-tailed_eagle_%28Haliaeetus_albicilla%29_defending_its_prey.jpg/440px-An_adult_white-tailed_eagle_%28Haliaeetus_albicilla%29_defending_its_prey.jpg 2x" data-file-width="6720" data-file-height="3780" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:An_adult_white-tailed_eagle_(Haliaeetus_albicilla)_defending_its_prey.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An adult white-tailed eagle with an apparent bird kill, of unidentified species. It is warding off <a href="/wiki/Carrion_crow" title="Carrion crow">crows</a> wishing to scavenge it.</div></div></div>
<p>White-tailed eagles are known to prey on about 170 species of bird, the most diverse group in their prey spectrum.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Willgohs_31-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willgohs-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Vrezec_98-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vrezec-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> While hunting birds, this massive, relatively slow-flying eagle requires an element of surprise, with often a tactful use of cover or bright sunlight upon the approach from a nearby perch.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Nadjafzadeh2_93-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nadjafzadeh2-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> For example, <a href="/wiki/Grey_heron" title="Grey heron">grey herons</a> (<i>Ardea cinerea</i>) have been caught after an eagle used a low flight over turbulent water to ambush them.<sup id="cite_ref-Willgohs_31-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willgohs-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> However, even with a stealthy attack, the <a href="/wiki/Waterfowl" class="mw-redirect" title="Waterfowl">waterfowl</a> favored in the avian diet tend to be highly wary and will more often than not escape.<sup id="cite_ref-Rudebeck_94-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rudebeck-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> The white-tailed eagles must then attack birds at times of vulnerability or injury, or will often utilize the prey's escape tactics against them.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Diving_duck" title="Diving duck">Diving ducks</a> and other diving <a href="/wiki/Water_bird" title="Water bird">water birds</a> will be taken preferentially where they are available. In hunting diving birds, they utilize a technique of forcing the birds to dive repeatedly to avoid attacks, until the victim is exhausted from the efforts and can then be caught. Usually while hunting like this, the white-tailed eagle tends to circle low to stay close to the intended victim, with birds diving in shallower water being preferred. Ducks with conspicuous plumage, such as male <a href="/wiki/Common_eider" title="Common eider">common eiders</a> (<i>Somateria mollissima</i>), with their pale plumage, may be easier to see under water and so may be taken somewhat more via this hunting method.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Willgohs_31-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willgohs-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Beyond waterfowl, both <a href="/wiki/Loon" title="Loon">loons</a> and <a href="/wiki/Grebe" title="Grebe">grebes</a> have been seen to be successfully hunted in this way.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Eagles were recorded doing between 7 and 12 attacks on <a href="/wiki/Eider" title="Eider">eiders</a> in <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> and were usually successful in procuring prey. Even as many as 65 passes have recorded in less than 45 minutes but more than a few attacks also start to exhaust the eagle, as one immature gave up after 15-28 attempts at a <a href="/wiki/Little_grebe" title="Little grebe">little grebe</a> (<i>Tachybaptus ruficollis</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-vonBlotzheim_88-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vonBlotzheim-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Flerov_71-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Flerov-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> While <a href="/wiki/Bald_eagle" title="Bald eagle">bald eagles</a> may attack diving ducks in the same way, they appear to do so somewhat less regularly and successfully.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Buehler_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Buehler-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> White-tailed eagles usually have less success hunting <a href="/wiki/Dabbling_duck" class="mw-redirect" title="Dabbling duck">dabbling ducks</a> because their normal predator response behavior is to take flight. In one instance, a <a href="/wiki/Mallard" title="Mallard">mallard</a> was caught while flying in mid-air, but usually the much larger eagle is unable to capture ducks in flight. While somewhat less swift in flight, healthy <a href="/wiki/Geese" class="mw-redirect" title="Geese">geese</a> can usually outpace a heavier eagle as well and one <a href="/wiki/Bean_goose" title="Bean goose">bean goose</a> (<i>Anser fabalis</i>) was even recorded to have defended itself successfully against an eagle's attack even though this goose was injured.<sup id="cite_ref-Rudebeck_94-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rudebeck-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> White-tailed eagles often hunt dabbling ducks and geese most successfully when they are <a href="/wiki/Molt" class="mw-redirect" title="Molt">molted</a> into their <a href="/wiki/Plumage" title="Plumage">eclipse plumage</a> which renders them temporarily flightless.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Swan" title="Swan">Swans</a> during winter may find themselves forced to land due to their bulk on a sheet of ice over water if they can find no open water, which can make their feet stuck to the ice. White-tailed eagles have been recorded utilizing this disability to attack and kill swans.<sup id="cite_ref-Fischer2_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fischer2-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> They've also been seen to attack numerous waterfowl when the birds are injured by buckshot from duck hunters.<sup id="cite_ref-Fischer2_132-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fischer2-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> Due to their status as enemy of other large birds, they are frequently <a href="/wiki/Mobbing_(animal_behavior)" title="Mobbing (animal behavior)">mobbed</a> by them and white-tailed eagles have been recorded utilizing violent mobbings to suddenly turn over in flight and predaceously grab one of the birds mobbing them, including large <a href="/wiki/Gull" title="Gull">gulls</a> and even a <a href="/wiki/Northern_goshawk" title="Northern goshawk">northern goshawk</a> (<i>Accipiter gentilis</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Meinertzhagen_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meinertzhagen-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> As an opportunistic predator, it often takes young birds freely as well as adult and fledged juvenile birds. In general, due to different nesting situations, white-tailed eagles instead of dabbling or diving water birds usually attack the more conspicuous or open nests of <a href="/wiki/Gull" title="Gull">gulls</a>, those of several other types of <a href="/wiki/Seabird" title="Seabird">seabird</a>, large <a href="/wiki/Corvid" class="mw-redirect" title="Corvid">corvids</a> or other accipitrids.<sup id="cite_ref-Uttendorfer_96-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Uttendorfer-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>, up to 86% of <a href="/wiki/Gull" title="Gull">gulls</a> taken were nestlings and juveniles.<sup id="cite_ref-Fischer1_114-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fischer1-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> Cases of white-tailed eagles eating eggs, instead of nestlings or older birds, is considered rare. Nonetheless, they have been recorded eating a few eggs, which they may carry in their beaks rather in their feet, of some seabirds such as <a href="/wiki/Kittiwake" title="Kittiwake">kittiwakes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eider" title="Eider">eiders</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cormorant" title="Cormorant">cormorants</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gull" title="Gull">gulls</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-vonBlotzheim_88-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vonBlotzheim-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Fischer2_132-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fischer2-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The most widely recorded avian prey species and, second most widely recorded prey species behind the pike, is the 1.14&#160;kg (2.5&#160;lb) <a href="/wiki/Mallard" title="Mallard">mallard</a>, due to its circumpolar range and commonality in many wetlands areas.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CRC_39-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Vrezec_98-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vrezec-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> However, as aforementioned, healthy mallards are difficult for white-tailed eagles due to their tendency to fly at first sign of danger. However, exploiting the mallard's flightlessness during eclipse plumage may result in eagles hunting them intensely only in late summer.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rudebeck_94-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rudebeck-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> Due to this mallards are usually a secondary prey species year around. The largest known representation of mallards in the diet were from <a href="/wiki/M%C3%BCritz_National_Park" title="Müritz National Park">Müritz National Park</a>, <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>, where mallards were the 3rd best represented prey species at 10.1% of 247 items and from <a href="/wiki/August%C3%B3w_Primeval_Forest" title="Augustów Primeval Forest">Augustów Primeval Forest</a>, <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a>, where mallards were the 2nd most numerous prey and made up 9.84% of 803 items.<sup id="cite_ref-Vrezec_98-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vrezec-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Fischer1_114-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fischer1-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> Taken more preferentially where they occur are <a href="/wiki/Common_eider" title="Common eider">common eiders</a>. When hunting eiders, perhaps the largest of diving ducks at a mean weight of 2.06&#160;kg (4.5&#160;lb), white-tailed eagles frequently force the eider to dive repeatedly until it is exhausted and can be captured.<sup id="cite_ref-Willgohs_31-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willgohs-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CRC_39-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Flerov_71-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Flerov-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> When sitting on the nest, the female common eider will try to escape in flight but is a relatively weak and ponderous flier and so too may be often victimized by the eagles.<sup id="cite_ref-vonBlotzheim_88-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vonBlotzheim-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> Otherwise the pale plumage of adult male common eiders while they're diving is reported to make them more vulnerable to eagle attacks.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Willgohs_31-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willgohs-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Eiders were the leading prey species in <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a> making 18.8% of 1612 prey items, as well as in the <a href="/wiki/%C3%85land_Islands" title="Åland Islands">Åland Islands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a> where the eider comprised 18.63% of 5161 prey items (thus nearly a thousand eiders were taken here).<sup id="cite_ref-Willgohs_31-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willgohs-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Vrezec_98-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vrezec-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ekblad_115-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ekblad-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> Eiders also appeared to be the main prey species in <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ingólfsson_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ingólfsson-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> There is evidence that a growing white-tailed eagle population is having a net negative effect on eider numbers in some areas, and locally eiders have altered to partial nocturnal foraging apparently to avoid hunting eagles.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hipfner_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hipfner-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> In inland regions, an avian prey species preferred by white-tailed eagles is the 836&#160;g (1.843&#160;lb) <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_coot" title="Eurasian coot">Eurasian coot</a> (<i>Fulica atra</i>). The coot is the second most widely represented bird prey species (and fourth species of any class known overall) in 18 dietary studies.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CRC_39-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Vrezec_98-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vrezec-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> Coots bunch together in marshy spots when approached by a flying eagle and as many as 5 eagles at once have been recorded attacking large flocks on the water.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Coots behaviour often endangers them to large raptors: they seldom dive, are weaker and slower fliers than most water birds and are collectively often less wary and more approachable than most waterfowl are.<sup id="cite_ref-Rudebeck_94-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rudebeck-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Valero_92-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Valero-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Nadjafzadeh2_93-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nadjafzadeh2-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> Coot were strongly the dominant food in <a href="/wiki/Wigry_National_Park" title="Wigry National Park">Wigry National Park</a>, <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a> where they made up 44.1% of 299 items, and were also the leading prey in <a href="/wiki/August%C3%B3w_Primeval_Forest" title="Augustów Primeval Forest">Augustów Primeval Forest</a>, <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a> where they made up 11.59% of the foods. Overall at Wigry and Augustów, birds altogether made up 66.2% and 47.83% of the diets, respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-Vrezec_98-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vrezec-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Zawadzka_104-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zawadzka-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Danube_Delta" title="Danube Delta">Danube Delta</a>, <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a>, birds climbed in importance of the diet from 21% in 1970 to 50% by 2015, thanks largely to increased numbers of coots.<sup id="cite_ref-Fischer2_132-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fischer2-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sandor_128-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sandor-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Somateria_mollissima_-_Holzbr%C3%BCcke_2011-03-24_15-21-26.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Somateria_mollissima_-_Holzbr%C3%BCcke_2011-03-24_15-21-26.JPG/220px-Somateria_mollissima_-_Holzbr%C3%BCcke_2011-03-24_15-21-26.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Somateria_mollissima_-_Holzbr%C3%BCcke_2011-03-24_15-21-26.JPG/330px-Somateria_mollissima_-_Holzbr%C3%BCcke_2011-03-24_15-21-26.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Somateria_mollissima_-_Holzbr%C3%BCcke_2011-03-24_15-21-26.JPG/440px-Somateria_mollissima_-_Holzbr%C3%BCcke_2011-03-24_15-21-26.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2925" data-file-height="2192" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Somateria_mollissima_-_Holzbr%C3%BCcke_2011-03-24_15-21-26.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Swimming male <a href="/wiki/Common_eider" title="Common eider">common eiders</a> are a frequent quarry of white-tailed eagles.</div></div></div>
<p>In total, about 38 species of <a href="/wiki/Waterfowl" class="mw-redirect" title="Waterfowl">waterfowl</a> are known to be hunted, as well as all available species of <a href="/wiki/Loon" title="Loon">loons</a> and <a href="/wiki/Grebe" title="Grebe">grebes</a>, several types of <a href="/wiki/Rail_(bird)" title="Rail (bird)">rails</a>, <a href="/wiki/Procellariiformes" title="Procellariiformes">tubenoses</a> as well as <a href="/wiki/Heron" title="Heron">herons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stork" title="Stork">storks</a> and other assorted large waders. White-tailed eagles also are known to hunt some 42 species of <a href="/wiki/Shorebird" class="mw-redirect" title="Shorebird">shorebird</a>, most significantly <a href="/wiki/Gull" title="Gull">gulls</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alcid" class="mw-redirect" title="Alcid">alcids</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Vrezec_98-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vrezec-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ekblad_115-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ekblad-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> Even shorebirds as small as 21.1&#160;g (0.74&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Little_stint" title="Little stint">little stint</a> (<i>Calidris minuta</i>), 62.6&#160;g (2.21&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Wood_sandpiper" title="Wood sandpiper">wood sandpiper</a> (<i>Tringa glareola</i>) and 64&#160;g (2.3&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Common_ringed_plover" title="Common ringed plover">common ringed plover</a> (<i>Charadrius hiaticula</i>) are known to be preyed upon, albeit quite rarely.<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_39-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sulkava_103-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sulkava-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> More than a dozen gulls are known in the prey spectrum from the <a href="/wiki/Little_gull" title="Little gull">smallest</a> to all four largest extant species.<sup id="cite_ref-Wille_99-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wille-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sulkava_103-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sulkava-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mlíkovský_97-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mlíkovský-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>, <a href="/wiki/Northern_fulmar" title="Northern fulmar">northern fulmar</a> (<i>Fulmarus glacialis</i>) are noted as a common prey species and as such may contribute to locally high levels of DDT and PCB chemicals in nesting eagles.<sup
