id="cite_ref-rspb_95-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rspb-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> However, fulmars defend themselves by regurgitating a smelly, tar-like oily substance that can impair the flight of predators and may even kill some intended predators when it is in large quantity, and young juvenile eagles, being less cautious and experienced, are most prone to being severely “oiled”.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> Alcids such as <a href="/wiki/Murre" class="mw-redirect" title="Murre">murres</a> tend to become especially important in the diet of eagles in coastal Norway during winter, especially near offshore islands, when coastal fish tend to move to deeper waters.<sup id="cite_ref-Willgohs_31-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willgohs-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hipfner_138-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hipfner-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> At least 8 species of dabbling duck are known in the prey spectrum. Due to the social inclination of dabbling ducks, they perhaps have the most success hunting isolated birds but they've also been taken from panic-stricken flocks as well.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Fischer2_132-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fischer2-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> Despite the difficulty of taking them, dabbling ducks of unidentified species were found to be the main food of white-tailed eagles in <a href="/wiki/Lake_Baikal" title="Lake Baikal">Lake Baikal</a>, where they comprised 51.8% of 199 prey items.<sup id="cite_ref-Mlíkovský_97-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mlíkovský-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mlíkovský2_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mlíkovský2-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Fennoscandia" title="Fennoscandia">Fennoscandia</a>, they are attracted to coastal waters during winter to attack large numbers of diving ducks including eiders, <a href="/wiki/Common_goldeneye" title="Common goldeneye">common goldeneye</a> (<i>Bucephala clangula</i>), <a href="/wiki/Common_merganser" title="Common merganser">common</a> (<i>Mergus merganser</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Red-breasted_merganser" title="Red-breasted merganser">red-breasted mergansers</a> (<i>Mergus serrator</i>), <a href="/wiki/Tufted_duck" title="Tufted duck">tufted ducks</a> (<i>Aythya fuligula</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Scoter" title="Scoter">scoters</a>. Year around in the <a href="/wiki/%C3%85land_Islands" title="Åland Islands">Åland Islands</a>, 66.2% of 5161 food items were birds, while in the 3 sites in different parts of <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a> birds made up 51.1% of 3152 food items.<sup id="cite_ref-Sulkava_103-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sulkava-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bergman_107-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bergman-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ekblad_115-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ekblad-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>, 52.4% of 1637 prey items were birds, mostly coots and unidentified waterfowl. More locally in Germany, in <a href="/wiki/M%C3%BCritz_National_Park" title="Müritz National Park">Müritz National Park</a> the percentage of birds in the diet climbs to 65.73%<sup id="cite_ref-Fischer1_114-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fischer1-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Vrezec_98-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vrezec-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>  Birds were strongly dominant in food records from Scotland, making up 73.53% of 1930 prey items, and in <a href="/wiki/Kandalaksha_Nature_Reserve" title="Kandalaksha Nature Reserve">Kandalaksha Nature Reserve</a>, where they comprised 75% of 523 prey items.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Flerov_71-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Flerov-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> 
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Haliaetus_albicilla_EM1B6120_(35944276502).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Haliaetus_albicilla_EM1B6120_%2835944276502%29.jpg/220px-Haliaetus_albicilla_EM1B6120_%2835944276502%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="141" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Haliaetus_albicilla_EM1B6120_%2835944276502%29.jpg/330px-Haliaetus_albicilla_EM1B6120_%2835944276502%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Haliaetus_albicilla_EM1B6120_%2835944276502%29.jpg/440px-Haliaetus_albicilla_EM1B6120_%2835944276502%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1938" data-file-height="1245" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Haliaetus_albicilla_EM1B6120_(35944276502).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Juvenile white-tailed eagle pursuing two <a href="/wiki/Northern_lapwing" title="Northern lapwing">northern lapwings</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>While most of the aforementioned water birds are modest of size and taken largely due to ease (diving water birds, whether healthy or infirm, and usually infirm or molting dabbling water birds), white-tailed eagles routinely attack larger water birds as well. In many areas, large numbers of 3.31&#160;kg (7.3&#160;lb) <a href="/wiki/Graylag_geese" class="mw-redirect" title="Graylag geese">graylag geese</a> (<i>Anser anser</i>), Europe's largest native wild goose, are taken. For example, they were the main prey, making up 28.2% of 192 prey items, for wintering eagles in <a href="/wiki/Oostvaardersplassen" title="Oostvaardersplassen">Oostvaardersplassen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>, and the 2nd most often recorded prey species in both <a href="/wiki/M%C3%BCritz_National_Park" title="Müritz National Park">Müritz National Park</a>, <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>, where they made up 16.42% of 247 prey items, and in <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austria</a>, where they made up 9.5% of 349 items.<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_39-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rijn_73-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rijn-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Vrezec_98-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vrezec-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Probst_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Probst-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> White-tailed eagles are known to prey on at least 10 species of geese, ranging in size from the 1.23&#160;kg (2.7&#160;lb) <a href="/wiki/Red-breasted_goose" title="Red-breasted goose">red-breasted goose</a> (<i>Branta ruficollis</i>) to the non-native 3.69&#160;kg (8.1&#160;lb) <a href="/wiki/Canada_goose" title="Canada goose">Canada goose</a> (<i>Branta canadensis</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_39-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> They will take many goslings during summer, as greylag goslings alone can comprise up 23% of the seasonal bird prey, and fully-grown geese in other seasons.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Fischer2_132-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fischer2-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> Large waders are taken when possible, including a half dozen <a href="/wiki/Heron" title="Heron">heron</a> species, and, larger still, both the young and adults of 5.5&#160;kg (12&#160;lb) <a href="/wiki/Common_crane" title="Common crane">common cranes</a> (<i>Grus grus</i>) and both the 2.93&#160;kg (6.5&#160;lb) <a href="/wiki/Black_stork" title="Black stork">black</a> and the 3.44&#160;kg (7.6&#160;lb) <a href="/wiki/White_stork" title="White stork">white stork</a> (<i>Ciconia ciconia</i>). Black and white storks are primary prey species in the <a href="/wiki/Polesie_State_Radioecological_Reserve" title="Polesie State Radioecological Reserve">Polesie State Radioecological Reserve</a>, <a href="/wiki/Belarus" title="Belarus">Belarus</a> where they comprised 12.6% (2nd most regular prey species) and 6.3% (4th most regular) of the diet, respectively. Large numbers of black stork were also taken in <a href="/wiki/August%C3%B3w_Primeval_Forest" title="Augustów Primeval Forest">Augustów Primeval Forest</a> where nearly 50 were found around eagle nests.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Vrezec_98-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vrezec-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sulkava_103-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sulkava-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Yurko_119-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yurko-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> They are reported to have attacked and eaten the largest seabirds they encounter, such as <a href="/wiki/Great_cormorant" title="Great cormorant">great cormorants</a> (<i>Phalacrocorax carbo</i>) and in some cases, such as in the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_sea" class="mw-redirect" title="Baltic sea">Baltic sea</a>, have nearly destroyed whole colonies, from the eggs to the adults which average about 2.57&#160;kg (5.7&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_39-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-hs.fi_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hs.fi-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Estonia" title="Estonia">Estonian</a> island of <a href="/wiki/Hiiumaa" title="Hiiumaa">Hiiumaa</a>, home to at least 25 pairs of sea eagles, as many as 26 individuals have been observed simultaneously culling a single cormorant colony.<sup id="cite_ref-Hiiumaa_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hiiumaa-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> Similarly large numbers were taken of the 2.82&#160;kg (6.2&#160;lb) <a href="/wiki/Japanese_cormorant" title="Japanese cormorant">Japanese cormorant</a> (<i>Phalacrocorax capillatus</i>), which was the second most numerous prey species, making up 11.63% of 533 prey items in Hokkaido, and opportunistically, when their <a href="/wiki/North_Atlantic" class="mw-redirect" title="North Atlantic">north Atlantic</a> colonies are accessed, great numbers of 3&#160;kg (6.6&#160;lb) <a href="/wiki/Northern_gannet" title="Northern gannet">northern gannets</a> (<i>Morus bassanus</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_39-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mori_126-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mori-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Whitfield_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whitfield-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> Vagrant white-tailed eagles in <a href="/wiki/Hawaii" title="Hawaii">Hawaii</a> were recorded to prey on several <a href="/wiki/Laysan_albatross" title="Laysan albatross">Laysan albatross</a> (<i>Phoebastria immutabilis</i>) and were suspected to prey on <a href="/wiki/Black-footed_albatross" title="Black-footed albatross">black-footed albatross</a> (<i>Phoebastria nigripes</i>), both weighing about 3.17&#160;kg (7.0&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_39-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> Another large water bird taken as adults are 4.98&#160;kg (11.0&#160;lb) <a href="/wiki/Common_loon" title="Common loon">common loons</a> (<i>Gavia immer</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CRC_39-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> However, the largest water birds they are known to kill are adult <a href="/wiki/Swan" title="Swan">swans</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Mute_swan" title="Mute swan">mute</a> (<i>Cygnus olor</i>), <a href="/wiki/Whooper_swan" title="Whooper swan">whooper</a> (<i>Cygnus cygnus</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Bewick%27s_swan" class="mw-redirect" title="Bewick&#39;s swan">Bewick's swans</a> (<i>Cygnus columbianus bewickii</i>). While cygnets and disabled birds (either by natural conditions such as ice or by human hunters) are at the greatest risk for eagle predation, white-tailed eagles have prey upon even healthy adult swans weighing at least 10&#160;kg (22&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Vrezec_98-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vrezec-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Zawadzka_104-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zawadzka-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>While land birds are a more infrequently part of the diet, at least 60 species have been recorded in the white-tailed eagles prey spectrum.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Vrezec_98-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vrezec-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> For the most part, land birds are taken so infrequently as to not warrant much mention. However, variable numbers of <a href="/wiki/Gamebird" class="mw-redirect" title="Gamebird">gamebirds</a> are taken opportunistically, and in <a href="/wiki/Belarus" title="Belarus">Belarus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, especially around the <a href="/wiki/White_Sea" title="White Sea">White Sea</a> where <a href="/wiki/Ptarmigan" class="mw-redirect" title="Ptarmigan">ptarmigan</a> are not infrequent prey, they will take a few <a href="/wiki/Grouse" title="Grouse">grouse</a>. White-tailed eagles are known to prey on some numbers of <a href="/wiki/Ring-necked_pheasant" class="mw-redirect" title="Ring-necked pheasant">ring-necked pheasant</a> (<i>Phasianus colchicus</i>) in <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austria</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Flerov_71-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Flerov-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ivanovsky_125-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ivanovsky-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Probst_143-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Probst-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> They will at times attack adult male <a href="/wiki/Western_capercaillie" title="Western capercaillie">western capercaillie</a> (<i>Tetrao urogallus</i>), of note for their large size at 4.2&#160;kg (9.3&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-Adrianova_118-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Adrianova-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> However, this is dwarfed by the largest avian prey credited to a white-tailed eagle, an adult male <a href="/wiki/Great_bustard" title="Great bustard">great bustard</a> (<i>Otis tarda</i>), which weighed an estimated 15&#160;kg (33&#160;lb) (which, much like exceptionally large fish taken, must have been consumed in the killing spot or subsequently dismantled as too large to fly with).<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Among the land birds taken, more than 20 <a href="/wiki/Passerine" title="Passerine">passerines</a> are included in the prey spectrum but most are obviously too small and swift to be anything but incidental prey.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> The smallest avian prey known for white-tailed eagles was a <a href="/wiki/Great_tit" title="Great tit">great tit</a> (<i>Parus major</i>), a species which weighs 16.4&#160;g (0.58&#160;oz) on average.<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_39-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> On one hand, small bird prey may be under-recorded since they leave few conspicuous remains but, on the other, are unlikely worthy of much pursuit as they have little food value. However, in one case, a white-tailed eagle was seen to fly into a <a href="/wiki/Flocking_(behavior)" title="Flocking (behavior)">murmuration</a> of <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_starling" class="mw-redirect" title="Eurasian starling">Eurasian starlings</a> (<i>Sturnus vulgaris</i>) and come away with a starling in hand.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Willgohs_31-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willgohs-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> The only passerine family taken in numbers would be the larger <a href="/wiki/Corvid" class="mw-redirect" title="Corvid">corvids</a>, of which 8 species are known in the prey spectrum.<sup id="cite_ref-Sulkava_103-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sulkava-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sandor_128-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sandor-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Samago_120-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Samago-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Hokkaido" title="Hokkaido">Hokkaido</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>, two species of corvid were well-represented in the diet, the 519.5&#160;g (1.145&#160;lb) <a href="/wiki/Large-billed_crow" title="Large-billed crow">large-billed crow</a> (<i>Corvus macrorhynchos</i>) and the 570&#160;g (1.26&#160;lb) <a href="/wiki/Carrion_crow" title="Carrion crow">carrion crow</a> (<i>Corvus corone</i>), which together comprised 14.8% of 533 prey items.<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_39-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mori_126-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mori-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mammals_and_other_prey">Mammals and other prey</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=White-tailed_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Mammals and other prey">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:British_birds_with_their_nests_and_eggs_(1896)_(14747475321).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/British_birds_with_their_nests_and_eggs_%281896%29_%2814747475321%29.jpg/220px-British_birds_with_their_nests_and_eggs_%281896%29_%2814747475321%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="149" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/British_birds_with_their_nests_and_eggs_%281896%29_%2814747475321%29.jpg/330px-British_birds_with_their_nests_and_eggs_%281896%29_%2814747475321%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/British_birds_with_their_nests_and_eggs_%281896%29_%2814747475321%29.jpg/440px-British_birds_with_their_nests_and_eggs_%281896%29_%2814747475321%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2368" data-file-height="1602" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:British_birds_with_their_nests_and_eggs_(1896)_(14747475321).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>1896 rendering of a white-tailed eagle with <a href="/wiki/European_rabbit" title="European rabbit">rabbit</a> prey.</div></div></div>
<p>Mammals are usually a quite secondary component of the diet. Although usually better represented than other non-fish and bird prey, their regional importance is variable.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> In known dietary studies, the mammalian contribution can vary from 0.49% to 41% of prey by number.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Flerov_71-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Flerov-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Vrezec_98-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vrezec-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> When mammals are most significant to the diet, it usually due to local white-tailed eagles preying on <a href="/wiki/Leporidae" title="Leporidae">rabbits and hares</a> with some regularity. In some studies from Scotland, the <a href="/wiki/European_rabbit" title="European rabbit">European rabbit</a> (<i>Oryctolagus cuniculus</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Mountain_hare" title="Mountain hare">mountain hare</a> (<i>Lepus timidus</i>) were found to contribute as much as nearly 25% of the diet.<sup id="cite_ref-Whitfield_148-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whitfield-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Halley_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Halley-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Watson2_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson2-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> A similar contribution of <a href="/wiki/European_hare" title="European hare">European hares</a> (<i>Lepus europaeus</i>) was found in the diet from <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austria</a>, where hares were the leading prey species making up 24.35% of 349 prey items (with mammals making up 34.67% of the overall diet).<sup id="cite_ref-Probst_143-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Probst-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> In studies from <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>, European hares were taken fairly often but were numerically secondary prey.<sup id="cite_ref-Vrezec_98-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vrezec-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Fischer1_114-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fischer1-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> Given that these rabbits and hares have average mature weights from 1.8&#160;kg (4.0&#160;lb) in rabbits to 3.8 and 4.2&#160;kg (8.4 and 9.3&#160;lb) in European and <a href="/wiki/Arctic_hare" title="Arctic hare">Arctic hares</a> (<i>Lepus arcticus</i>), they can make a hearty contribution to the prey biomass when available (although certainly juveniles are attacked as much if not more so than adult rabbits and hares).<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Whitfield_148-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whitfield-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Halley_153-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Halley-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> Other large mammalian prey can include <a href="/wiki/Fox" title="Fox">foxes</a>, with 3.2&#160;kg (7.1&#160;lb) <a href="/wiki/Arctic_fox" title="Arctic fox">Arctic foxes</a> (<i>Vulpes lagopus</i>) being the 6th most regular prey species in <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a> and about a dozen 5.77&#160;kg (12.7&#160;lb) <a href="/wiki/Red_fox" title="Red fox">red foxes</a> (<i>Vulpes vulpes</i>) recorded as taken or scavenged in <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a> (predaceous attacks on adult red foxes have been reportedly witnessed).<sup id="cite_ref-Willgohs_31-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willgohs-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wille_99-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wille-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> In both the offshore islands of Norway, where Arctic hare can be important prey, and Greenland, where the Arctic fox are prey, large numbers of both are taken when their pelage remains white but snow melts prematurely.<sup id="cite_ref-Willgohs_31-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willgohs-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wille_99-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wille-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> A white-tailed eagle was once observed to kill and feed on a live adult <a href="/wiki/Golden_jackal" title="Golden jackal">golden jackal</a> (<i>Canis auereus</i>) that was trapped in a furbearer's trap.<sup id="cite_ref-Bannerman_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bannerman-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> White-tailed eagles are known to prey on seal pups but most are likely sickly and perhaps both adult and pup seals are most likely to be eaten as carrion. Four <a href="/wiki/Baikal_seal" title="Baikal seal">Baikal seal</a> (<i>Pusa sibirica</i>) pups were taken in <a href="/wiki/Lake_Baikal" title="Lake Baikal">Lake Baikal</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Mlíkovský_97-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mlíkovský-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> They are considered a predator even for live <a href="/wiki/Grey_seal" title="Grey seal">grey seal</a> (<i>Halichoerus grypus</i>) pups that weigh 11.75&#160;kg (25.9&#160;lb) at birth.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> In one instance, a white-tailed eagle reportedly tried to attack an adult <a href="/wiki/Harbor_seal" title="Harbor seal">harbor seal</a> (<i>Phoca vitulina</i>) but was immediately dragged into the water and soon surfaced dying with a broken wing.<sup id="cite_ref-Bannerman_162-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bannerman-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> Similarly, in one anecdote, an eagle drowned while apparently attacking an adult <a href="/wiki/Harbor_porpoise" class="mw-redirect" title="Harbor porpoise">harbor porpoise</a> (<i>Phocoena phocoena</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Willgohs_31-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willgohs-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> At least 7 species of <a href="/wiki/Mustelid" class="mw-redirect" title="Mustelid">mustelid</a> have been recorded in the white-tailed eagle's diet, from the <a href="/wiki/Least_weasel" title="Least weasel">smallest weasel</a> to, rarely, even adult <a href="/wiki/European_otter" class="mw-redirect" title="European otter">European otters</a> (<i>Lutra lutra</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Arkadevich_164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arkadevich-164">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> Another mustelid, the <a href="/wiki/American_mink" title="American mink">American mink</a> (<i>Neovison vison</i>) was introduced as a furbearer to <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a> but then became an invasive pest, as a fast-breeding killer that threatens many native species. In turn, Finnish white-tailed eagles have become the main natural control and may inhibit the mink from breeding via heavy predation. Conversely, the white-tailed eagle have not been known to prey on the critically endangered, native <a href="/wiki/European_mink" title="European mink">European mink</a> (<i>Mustela lutreola</i>) (perhaps due to its shier habits) which the American mink are known to have been outcompeting in some areas (but the European has mainly declined due to massive overhunting by humans as a furbearer).<sup id="cite_ref-Salo_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Salo-165">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> Kits of larger mammals such as <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_beaver" title="Eurasian beaver">Eurasian</a> (<i>Castor fiber</i>) and <a href="/wiki/North_American_beaver" title="North American beaver">North American beavers</a> (<i>Castor canadensis</i>) (introduced) and of <a href="/wiki/European_badger" title="European badger">European badger</a> (<i>Meles meles</i>) have been found as prey.<sup id="cite_ref-Yurko_119-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yurko-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Merikotkas_kanarbikus.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Merikotkas_kanarbikus.jpg/220px-Merikotkas_kanarbikus.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Merikotkas_kanarbikus.jpg/330px-Merikotkas_kanarbikus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Merikotkas_kanarbikus.jpg/440px-Merikotkas_kanarbikus.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Merikotkas_kanarbikus.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>White-tailed eagle adult on the island of <a href="/wiki/Hiiumaa" title="Hiiumaa">Hiiumaa</a> in <a href="/wiki/Estonia" title="Estonia">Estonia</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The most widely reported mammalian prey known (albeit usually as a supplemental prey species) in the non-native <a href="/wiki/Muskrat" title="Muskrat">muskrat</a> (<i>Ondatra zibethicus</i>), which averages about 1.1&#160;kg (2.4&#160;lb), and may be taken from “muskrat farms” or feral wetland populations. While largely a supplemental prey item, a respectable number of 137 were recorded in the diet from Finland.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sulkava_103-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sulkava-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> Also, a study on the <a href="/wiki/Ili_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Ili River">Ili delta</a> in <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a> has shown up to 30-43% of prey remains at eyries to consist of muskrat in spring and fall but only 14% in summer (for unknown reasons).<sup id="cite_ref-vonBlotzheim_88-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vonBlotzheim-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> In total, about 20 <a href="/wiki/Rodent" title="Rodent">rodent</a> species are known in the white-tailed eagle's prey spectrum. In some of the coldest climes of <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, such as in the <a href="/wiki/Ural_mountains" class="mw-redirect" title="Ural mountains">Ural mountains</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Kola_peninsula" class="mw-redirect" title="Kola peninsula">Kola peninsula</a>, white-tailed eagles have been recorded to live on surprisingly high numbers of small rodents, comprising 13.8% and more than 21% of all prey remains, respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-Flerov_71-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Flerov-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Samago_120-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Samago-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> Even agile <a href="/wiki/Red_squirrel" title="Red squirrel">red squirrels</a> (<i>Sciurus vulgaris</i>) may be caught from trees in some cases.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup> The smallest rodents known in the prey spectrum are the 27.4&#160;g (0.97&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Common_vole" title="Common vole">common vole</a> (Microtus arvalis) and the 23.4&#160;g (0.83&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Wood_mouse" title="Wood mouse">wood mouse</a> (<i>Apodemus sylvaticus</i>) but mammalian prey down to the size of 8.1&#160;g (0.29&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Common_shrew" title="Common shrew">common shrews</a> (<i>Sorex araneus</i>), indeed the smallest vertebrate known to have been preyed upon, has been recorded.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Samago_120-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Samago-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> On the other end of the size scale from rodents, more than a dozen <a href="/wiki/Ungulate" title="Ungulate">ungulate</a> species have been found in the foods of white-tailed eagles, but a very large proportion of this is likely from carrion found already dead.<sup id="cite_ref-Sulkava_103-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sulkava-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> Very large food sources such as <a href="/wiki/Horse" title="Horse">horses</a> (<i>Equus ferus caballus</i>), <a href="/wiki/Moose" title="Moose">moose</a> (<i>Alces alces</i>), <a href="/wiki/Cattle" title="Cattle">cattle</a> (<i>Bos taurus</i>) and <a href="/wiki/European_bison" title="European bison">European bison</a> (<i>Bison bosanus</i>) are certainly visited as carrion always.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rijn_73-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rijn-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sulkava_103-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sulkava-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> Wild ungulate species known to have their young attacked by white-tailed eagles in variable numbers may include deer such as <a href="/wiki/Reindeer" title="Reindeer">reindeer</a> (<i>Rangifer tarandus</i>), <a href="/wiki/Red_deer" title="Red deer">red deer</a> (<i>Cervus elaphus</i>) and <a href="/wiki/European_roe_deer" class="mw-redirect" title="European roe deer">European roe deer</a> (<i>Capreolus capreolus</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Wild_boar" title="Wild boar">wild boar</a> (<i>Sus scrofa</i>). Boar made up 7.1% of the prey remains at Polesski Reserve, Belarus.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Willgohs_31-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willgohs-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Yurko_119-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yurko-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> At least two successful attacks on adult roe deer have been reported, which could potentially weigh around 25.4&#160;kg (56&#160;lb) (their average mature weight), in addition to several cases of predation on young roe deer.<sup id="cite_ref-Willgohs_31-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willgohs-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup> A yearling juvenile male eagle scaling 5&#160;kg (11&#160;lb), newly reintroduced into the wild on <a href="/wiki/R%C3%B9m" title="Rùm">Rùm</a> killed a healthy, 7&#160;kg (15&#160;lb) red deer calf within a couple days.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> One of the primary causes of the white-tailed eagles persecution by humans is that white-tailed eagles often feed on <a href="/wiki/Domestic_sheep" class="mw-redirect" title="Domestic sheep">domestic sheep</a> (<i>Ovis aries</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Domestic_goat" class="mw-redirect" title="Domestic goat">goats</a> (<i>Capra aegagrus hircus</i>), especially lambs and kids. However, they very seldomly take any healthy specimens and mostly feed on these after death. Of 36 cases of feeding on lambs and kids by white-tailed eagles in <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a>, only 12 could be proven to have been taken alive by the eagles.<sup id="cite_ref-Willgohs_31-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willgohs-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Prey of other animal classes is rarely taken by white-tailed eagles. Particularly, the diversity of <a href="/wiki/Reptile" title="Reptile">reptiles</a> known in the prey spectrum, at only 6 species, is quite paltry when compared to the many species known to be hunted by the <a href="/wiki/Bald_eagle" title="Bald eagle">bald eagle</a>. Further among <a href="/wiki/Amphibian" title="Amphibian">amphibians</a> only two species of toad are known to be taken.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mori_126-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mori-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Samago_120-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Samago-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sherrod_178-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sherrod-178">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Ural_mountains" class="mw-redirect" title="Ural mountains">Ural mountains</a> is the only region where some diversity of reptile and amphibian species have been reported in their diet.<sup id="cite_ref-Samago_120-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Samago-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> In only the white-tailed eagles nesting in <a href="/wiki/Hokkaido" title="Hokkaido">Hokkaido</a>, they've been known to hunt large <a href="/wiki/Cephalopod" title="Cephalopod">cephalopods</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Robust_clubhook_squid" class="mw-redirect" title="Robust clubhook squid">robust clubhook squid</a> (<i>Onykia robusta</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Giant_Pacific_octopus" title="Giant Pacific octopus">giant Pacific octopus</a> (<i>Enteroctopus dofleini</i>), though likely mainly younger specimens and those collected and perhaps injured by large-scale fishing operations.<sup id="cite_ref-Mori_126-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mori-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> White-tailed eagle juveniles have been seen rarely foraging for unidentified <a href="/wiki/Clam" title="Clam">clams</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mussel" title="Mussel">mussels</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Rhine" title="Rhine">Rhine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup> However, the miscellaneous shells of marine mussels and snails found in Norway are probably usually consumed secondarily from the stomachs of eiders.<sup id="cite_ref-Willgohs_31-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willgohs-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> An exceptional number of <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insects</a>, amounting to 24% of food items by number, were found in the foods of white-tailed eagles in <a href="/wiki/August%C3%B3w_Primeval_Forest" title="Augustów Primeval Forest">Augustów Primeval Forest</a>, <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a>, almost entirely <a href="/wiki/Odonata" title="Odonata">Odonata</a>. The source of this food is not clear as the white-tailed eagle is far too large and bulky a raptor to invest much time in pursuit of insects.<sup id="cite_ref-Vrezec_98-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vrezec-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Interspecies_predatory_relationships">Interspecies predatory relationships</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=White-tailed_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Interspecies predatory relationships">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:White-tailed_Eagle_with_2_Buzzards.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/White-tailed_Eagle_with_2_Buzzards.jpg/220px-White-tailed_Eagle_with_2_Buzzards.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="154" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/White-tailed_Eagle_with_2_Buzzards.jpg/330px-White-tailed_Eagle_with_2_Buzzards.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/White-tailed_Eagle_with_2_Buzzards.jpg/440px-White-tailed_Eagle_with_2_Buzzards.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="560" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:White-tailed_Eagle_with_2_Buzzards.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A juvenile (right) being <a href="/wiki/Mobbing_(animal_behavior)" title="Mobbing (animal behavior)">mobbed</a> by a pair of <a href="/wiki/Common_buzzard" title="Common buzzard">common buzzards</a> over the <a href="/wiki/Canna,_Scotland" title="Canna, Scotland">Isle of Canna</a></div></div></div>
<p>As the largest eagle in the majority of its distribution, the white-tailed eagle is an <a href="/wiki/Apex_predator" title="Apex predator">apex predator</a> in its range.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Salo_165-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Salo-165">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> In some areas it may compete with other large raptors, especially <a href="/wiki/Golden_eagle" title="Golden eagle">golden eagles</a>. Relations between these two species are complex and variable.<sup id="cite_ref-Whitfield_148-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whitfield-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Halley_153-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Halley-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Watson_43-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>, where both eagles have been reintroduced or re-established, competition is considerable. This is, in part, due to their diets being more similar here than elsewhere in their range, with both species preying heavily on <a href="/wiki/Leporidae" title="Leporidae">rabbits and hares</a> and being reliant on carrion all year round, whereas in most other areas both favor carrion mostly only in winter. As much as 90% of their diets in Scotland can overlap. The white-tailed eagle elsewhere usually prefers fish and water birds.<sup id="cite_ref-Halley_153-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Halley-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Watson_43-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Another factor is the reintroduction of white-tailed eagles occurred after golden eagles had been re-established in Scotland and that some of the golden eagles began to nest beyond their usually rocky, mountainous habitats, in or near trees in coastal or near lowland wet areas historically occupied by white-tailed eagles.  More territorial conflicts between the species have been recorded in the last few decades.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Halley_153-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Halley-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> Given appropriate healthy populations and ample habitat, as in <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a>, the golden and white-tailed eagles are considerably segregated in nesting regions and direct competition is much milder. In spite of a size advantage for the white-tailed eagle, the golden eagle is reportedly “strongly dominant” over the white-tailed in food conflicts and, perhaps, in direct nesting competition. This is in part because of the swifter, more agile flight of golden eagles, as well as their greater overall aggression towards other raptors and perhaps due to their somewhat longer toes and larger talons, although there is little evidence that the golden and white-tailed eagles are notably different in strength.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Halley_153-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Halley-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Halley2_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Halley2-180">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> In many intraguild diurnal raptor assemblages, a slightly smaller, swifter-flying species often dominate their heavier competitors unless size differences become extreme,<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup> to the contrary of <a href="/wiki/Owl" title="Owl">owls</a> and several other predators which often adhere fairly strictly to a size-based dominance hierarchy.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-183">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola-184">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> Golden eagles won all food conflicts over carrion observed by one author over the course of two winters in Norway, with the white-tailed eagles only displacing goldens after the golden eagles had already fed for some time.<sup id="cite_ref-Halley_153-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Halley-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Halley2_180-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Halley2-180">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> However, cases of white-tailed eagles winning food conflicts have been reported as well and perhaps more assured mature white-tailed eagles may fare better in such conflicts.<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> Some territorial conflicts in Scotland have escalated, albeit rarely, to both eagle species killing the other.<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_43-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> White-tailed eagles are said to be “dominant”, however, in population ecology as they can exist at higher population densities and typically outnumber golden eagles because of their longer gut and more efficient digestive system, being able to live better with less food.<sup id="cite_ref-Whitfield_148-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whitfield-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Halley_153-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Halley-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Halley2_180-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Halley2-180">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> Notably, in competition in <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a>, neither the golden or <a href="/wiki/Bald_eagle" title="Bald eagle">bald eagle</a> are said to be dominant and either species may win conflicts. Furthermore, bald eagles may succeed in procuring up to 25% of fresh golden eagle kills in winter and golden eagles are reported to decidedly avoid active nesting areas of bald eagles.<sup id="cite_ref-Buehler_131-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Buehler-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Haliaetus_albicilla_6043_(16702793704).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Haliaetus_albicilla_6043_%2816702793704%29.jpg/220px-Haliaetus_albicilla_6043_%2816702793704%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Haliaetus_albicilla_6043_%2816702793704%29.jpg/330px-Haliaetus_albicilla_6043_%2816702793704%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Haliaetus_albicilla_6043_%2816702793704%29.jpg/440px-Haliaetus_albicilla_6043_%2816702793704%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1106" data-file-height="1106" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Haliaetus_albicilla_6043_(16702793704).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>White-tailed eagles often opportunistically steal food from other birds, especially fish from <a href="/wiki/Osprey" title="Osprey">ospreys</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Beyond golden eagles, white-tailed eagles may live alongside a wide range of other large raptors, but other eagles are considerably different in dietary and habitat preferences, so there is almost no competitive effect.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> For example, in <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a>, white-tailed eagles were recorded to nest in proximity to golden eagles, <a href="/wiki/Eastern_imperial_eagle" title="Eastern imperial eagle">eastern imperial eagles</a> (<i>Aquila heliaca</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Steppe_eagle" title="Steppe eagle">steppe eagles</a> (<i>Aquila nipalensis</i>) and all four eagles appear almost entirely indifferent to the presence of the other species, given their considerable partitioning in diet and habitat preferences. More significantly than the <i>Aquila</i> species, the white-tailed eagle seemed to have no issue nesting within a few hundred metres of the other eagles.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup> A more direct effect may be detected on other fish-eating birds, for example recovering numbers of white-tailed eagles in <a href="/wiki/Lithuania" title="Lithuania">Lithuania</a> was thought to limit local <a href="/wiki/Osprey" title="Osprey">osprey</a> populations. However, in similar habitats, <a href="/wiki/White_stork" title="White stork">white storks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lesser_spotted_eagle" title="Lesser spotted eagle">lesser spotted eagles</a> (<i>Clanga pomarina</i>) appeared to not be competitively effected.<sup id="cite_ref-Treinys2_192-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Treinys2-192">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup> Besides competitive effects, white-tailed eagles may adversely effect ospreys by habitually robbing them of their catches.<sup id="cite_ref-Willgohs_31-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willgohs-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Besides the poorly studied relations between the <a href="/wiki/Pallas%27s_fish_eagle" title="Pallas&#39;s fish eagle">Pallas's fish eagle</a> and white-tailed eagle in the southern limits of the latter's Asian distribution, the only other <i>Haliaeetus</i> to live broadly alongside white-tailed eagles are <a href="/wiki/Steller%27s_sea_eagle" title="Steller&#39;s sea eagle">Steller's sea eagles</a>. Being larger and heavier, the Steller's is often characterized as a more powerful predator.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brown_32-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Nonetheless, studies of the species ecologies show that the Steller's is a more restricted feeder on <a href="/wiki/Salmonid" class="mw-redirect" title="Salmonid">salmonids</a> and that the Steller's tended to defend a smaller home range area than white-tailed eagles do in the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Far_East" title="Russian Far East">Russian Far East</a>. However, the Steller's appeared to be more flexible in readily switching nest sites between trees and rocky areas, whereas white-tailed eagles of this area exclusively nested in trees. Both species seemed to avoid active territories of the other but little direct conflict during breeding was detected.<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup> Although the Steller's sea eagle may be somewhat favored in wintertime food conflicts given its sometimes considerably larger size, both it and the white-tailed eagles have been observed to win conflicts over fish, with golden eagles sometimes entering the fray and sometimes losing or winning conflicts.<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup> Given their larger population and farther current range into warmer areas (whereas the modern white-tailed eagle is only common in cold, northern climes), bald eagles (the ecological equivalent of the white-tailed eagle in North America) have a considerably broader prey spectrum than white-tailed eagles that ranges well over 400 species, with more species recorded from nearly all animal taxon. Although about 56% of the bald eagle's diet is comprised by fish, bald eagles often take a higher diversity and numbers of alternate prey such as mammals, reptiles and amphibians than do white-tailed eagles.<sup id="cite_ref-Sherrod_178-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sherrod-178">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Stalmaster_198-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stalmaster-198">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Although other birds of prey are seldom caught, given that they are wary, fast and can defend themselves well, the white-tailed eagle can be characterized as an opportunistic predator of such birds.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>  Indicating that they are regarded as a threat to raptors is that they are frequently mobbed by a wide range of raptor species when displaying active flights, to a similar regard that the perhaps more aggressively predatory <a href="/wiki/Golden_eagle" title="Golden eagle">golden eagle</a> is.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup> Given the difficulty of this prey type, white-tailed eagles are likely to attack other birds of prey when the victims are distracted, whether by migration on windy days, nesting duties or when trying to capture their own prey, are previously injured or they may even capture one while the raptor tries to mob the eagle. Certainly, nestlings and fledglings can certainly comprise a large fraction of the birds of prey caught as well as adult ones.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Meinertzhagen_133-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meinertzhagen-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Treinys2_192-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Treinys2-192">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup> Other accipitrids they are known to have preyed upon, in increasing order of size are the <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_sparrowhawk" title="Eurasian sparrowhawk">Eurasian sparrowhawk</a> (<i>Accipiter nisus</i>),<sup id="cite_ref-Sulkava_103-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sulkava-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Western_marsh_harrier" title="Western marsh harrier">western marsh harrier</a> (<i>Circus aeruginosus</i>),<sup id="cite_ref-Probst_143-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Probst-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Black_kite" title="Black kite">black kite</a> (<i>Milvus migrans</i>),<sup id="cite_ref-Mori_126-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mori-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/European_honey-buzzard" class="mw-redirect" title="European honey-buzzard">European honey-buzzard</a> (<i>Pernis apivorus</i>),<sup id="cite_ref-Sulkava_103-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sulkava-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>  <a href="/wiki/Common_buzzard" title="Common buzzard">common buzzard</a> (<i>Buteo buteo</i>), <a href="/wiki/Northern_goshawk" title="Northern goshawk">northern goshawk</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Sulkava_103-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sulkava-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Red_kite" title="Red kite">red kite</a> (<i>Milvus milvus</i>),<sup id="cite_ref-Palmer_111-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Palmer-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Lesser_spotted_eagle" title="Lesser spotted eagle">lesser spotted eagle</a> (<i>Clanga pomarina</i>)<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Eastern_imperial_eagle" title="Eastern imperial eagle">eastern imperial eagle</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Palmer_111-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Palmer-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> in addition to <a href="/wiki/Osprey" title="Osprey">osprey</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Sulkava_103-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sulkava-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> Non-accipitrid raptorial birds, these more likely restricted to fully independent birds due to their less conspicuous nests, known to fall prey to white-tailed eagles include <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_pygmy_owl" title="Eurasian pygmy owl">Eurasian pygmy owl</a> (<i>Glaucidium passerinum</i>),<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_183-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-183">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Boreal_owl" title="Boreal owl">boreal owl</a> (<i>Aegolius funereus</i>),<sup id="cite_ref-Sulkava_103-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sulkava-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_hobby" title="Eurasian hobby">Eurasian hobby</a> (<i>Falco subbuteo</i>),<sup id="cite_ref-Arkadevich_164-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arkadevich-164">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Long-eared_owl" title="Long-eared owl">long-eared owl</a> (Asio otus),<sup id="cite_ref-Ivanovsky_125-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ivanovsky-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Northern_hawk_owl" class="mw-redirect" title="Northern hawk owl">northern hawk owl</a> (<i>Surnia ulula</i>),<sup id="cite_ref-Sulkava_103-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sulkava-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Short-eared_owl" title="Short-eared owl">short-eared owl</a> (<i>Asio flammeus</i>),<sup id="cite_ref-Sulkava_103-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sulkava-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Peregrine_falcon" title="Peregrine falcon">peregrine falcon</a> (<i>Falco peregrinus</i>),<sup id="cite_ref-Sulkava_103-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sulkava-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Snowy_owl" title="Snowy owl">snowy owl</a> (<i>Bubo scandiacus</i>)<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_eagle_owl" class="mw-redirect" title="Eurasian eagle owl">Eurasian eagle owl</a> (<i>Bubo bubo</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola_184-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola-184">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> Accipitrid prey size ranges by body mass ranges from 237&#160;g (8.4&#160;oz) for a sparrowhawk to 3.13&#160;kg (6.9&#160;lb) in the imperial eagle and that of non-accipitrid raptors ranges from 58.5&#160;g (2.06&#160;oz) for the pygmy owl to 2.68&#160;kg (5.9&#160;lb) for the eagle owl and apparently both imperial eagles and eagle owls that were caught were adults.<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_39-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Palmer_111-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Palmer-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Voous_183-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-183">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup> As true apex predators, healthy adult white-tailed eagles have no known natural predators.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Treinys2_192-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Treinys2-192">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Breeding">Breeding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=White-tailed_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Breeding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Courtship_and_mating">Courtship and mating</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=White-tailed_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Courtship and mating">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<p>The breeding season is from January to July in the south of the white-tailed eagles range, and from April to September in the northern part of their range.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_9-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> They pair for life, though if one dies replacement can occur quickly. A bond is formed when a permanent home range is chosen. White-tailed eagles frequently engage during early spring variously in soaring, sky-dancing and other aerial displays, all with much loud calling, often performed by pair members together, including spectacular mutual cartwheel downwards where talons touch or interlock.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_9-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Helander2_205-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Helander2-205">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup> Nuptial displays may occur almost year-around in some parts of the range, increasing after young disperse in autumn, thence at rare intervals until spring when such behavior, of course, peaks.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_32-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Courtship often begins with a bird, often the male, "sky pointing" or "long calling" by throwing their head back. Soaring by pairs follows, with either partner leading by 1 to 6&#160;m (3.3 to 19.7&#160;ft) or soaring opposite directions. One may swoop upon the other who responds by tilting to one side or may roll over to touch talons momentarily before separating. This may be repeated or gain intensity until they are talon-grappling or "mutual cartwheeling", consisting of the pair locking talons mid-air and whirling earthwards in series of spectacular cartwheels. When doing this, the pair may stop only a few feet above the ground.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-vonBlotzheim_88-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vonBlotzheim-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Helander2_205-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Helander2-205">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup> Talon grappling is usually associated with territorial clashes in most accipitrids, especially between males, but in <i>Haliaeetus</i> such behaviour also seems to be related to courtship and is engaged by pairs as well as males attacking intruding males.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup> When locally common, 2-3 pairs can be seen displaying in the sky quite near each other but each pair in fact are within a border of a well-defined territory.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_32-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> In Greenland, recorded pair densities were 0.3-0.6 occupied territories per 100&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (39&#160;sq&#160;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup> In eastern Germany, densities were reported as 1.6-2 pairs per 100&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (39&#160;sq&#160;mi). Norwegian nests appear at no closer than 1–2&#160;km (0.62–1.24&#160;mi); territory size on Norwegian coasts were reported as roughly 6 to 9&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (2.3 to 3.5&#160;sq&#160;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-Willgohs_31-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willgohs-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Fischer noted 8-9 pairs on the small Norwegian island of <a href="/wiki/Fugloy" title="Fugloy">Fugloy</a> due to its huge seabird colonies, with late summer numbers boosting to 75 eagles, all within an area of only 22&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (8.5&#160;sq&#160;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-Fischer2_132-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fischer2-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> 5 occupied nests were noted on an 80&#160;km (50&#160;mi) stretch of the Russian <a href="/wiki/Yenisei_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Yenisei River">Yenisei River</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Fischer2_132-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fischer2-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> Mating chiefly occurs between mid-March and mid-April in most parts of the range. The pair may copulate be about once every 20 minutes for several hours. Prior to mating, a more skilled and graceful flying than usual may be undertaken by the male as the final stage of courtship.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_32-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> After his final display, the female crouches low, almost flat, with her head and neck outstretched, wings half open, and tail held level with the rest of the body. Mating has been recorded in white-tailed eagles to occur almost anywhere including a low perch, on the nest, on the ground or even on frozen lake surfaces, usually close to the nest at least but also at as far away from the nest as 3&#160;km (1.9&#160;mi) in Norway. Sometimes both members of the pair will assume the female's typical mating position simultaneously side-by-side until he jumps on her back. Once the male mounts he often calls loudly and flaps both wings to maintain his balance. After about 12 seconds he dismounts and sits quietly, ultimately will do so about 5 to 6 in 1.5 hours and at nearly any time of the day.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Willgohs_31-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willgohs-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-vonBlotzheim_88-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vonBlotzheim-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nest_characteristics">Nest characteristics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=White-tailed_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Nest characteristics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Haliaeetus_albicilla_empty_nest.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Haliaeetus_albicilla_empty_nest.jpg/220px-Haliaeetus_albicilla_empty_nest.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Haliaeetus_albicilla_empty_nest.jpg/330px-Haliaeetus_albicilla_empty_nest.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Haliaeetus_albicilla_empty_nest.jpg/440px-Haliaeetus_albicilla_empty_nest.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3296" data-file-height="2472" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Haliaeetus_albicilla_empty_nest.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A nest of a white-tailed eagle in <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a>. Despite the preferable location it is unoccupied, and likely an alternate nest.</div></div></div>
<p>White-tailed eagles most often nest in large trees, with perhaps coniferous trees preferred and nests may be in a high main fork, on the canopy or a large side branch.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_32-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Access to food is a key consideration in the nest's location in white-tailed eagles. In Germany, 75% of eyries were within 3&#160;km (1.9&#160;mi) of a lake, although one was 11&#160;km (6.8&#160;mi) away from any lake though was somewhat closer to the Baltic Sea.<sup id="cite_ref-Oehme_209-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oehme-209">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup> 80% of German nests were described as being in forests but eagles exhibited preferences for wooded islands or promontories and most nests were usually towards edge of open space: a clearing, marshy ground or even agricultural land. Among the few eyries in open land were never more than 150&#160;m (490&#160;ft) away from woods. Only 1 cliff nest was recorded in Germany, on the Baltic island of <a href="/wiki/Rugen" class="mw-redirect" title="Rugen">Rugen</a>. Older records indicated ground nests also recorded in the German Baltic region. Most German nests were at heights from 2 to 10&#160;m (6.6 to 32.8&#160;ft) off ground in the main fork of trees, rarely on ample horizontal tree branches.<sup id="cite_ref-Fischer2_132-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fischer2-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Oehme_209-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oehme-209">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup> Tree nest height is partially based on tree species, i.e. in <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a>, on <a href="/wiki/Populus_nigra" title="Populus nigra">black poplars</a> (<i>Populus nigra</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Willow" title="Willow">willows</a> nest height would was recorded at 15 to 25&#160;m (49 to 82&#160;ft) of the ground but one was on a broken tree at only 7&#160;m (23&#160;ft) from the ground. There in the lower Danube of Romania beyond the mainly utilized black poplars and willows, one was on "a weak oak sapling", 6 on high, thick <a href="/wiki/Oak" title="Oak">oaks</a>, 5 in <a href="/wiki/Populus_alba" title="Populus alba">white poplars</a> (<i>Populus alba</i>), 2 in <a href="/wiki/Beech" title="Beech">beeches</a> and one in a wild <a href="/wiki/Pear" title="Pear">pear</a> tree.<sup id="cite_ref-Bannerman_162-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bannerman-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> Another quote on eyrie height along the Danube was at 19 to 33&#160;m (62 to 108&#160;ft) above the ground.<sup id="cite_ref-vonBlotzheim_88-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vonBlotzheim-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> Trees used for nests in <a href="/wiki/Hokkaido" title="Hokkaido">Hokkaido</a> were often the tallest in a stand, averaging 2.8&#160;m (9.2&#160;ft) higher than the average tree height in their stand. Tree species preferred here were <i><a href="/wiki/Picea_glehnii" title="Picea glehnii">Picea glehnii</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Alnus_japonica" title="Alnus japonica">Alnus japonica</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup> Nest height in Hokkaido was similar to that in Europe at 16.5 to 25&#160;m (54 to 82&#160;ft) above the ground.<sup id="cite_ref-Mori_126-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mori-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> In eastern Germany, among 177 nests, 65% were in mature pines, 22% in <a href="/wiki/Fagus_sylvatica" title="Fagus sylvatica">copper beeches</a> (<i>Fagus sylvatica</i>), 8% in <a href="/wiki/Oak" title="Oak">oak</a> with the remainder in <a href="/wiki/Alder" title="Alder">alder</a>, <a href="/wiki/Elm" title="Elm">elm</a>, <a href="/wiki/Populus" title="Populus">poplar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Birch" title="Birch">birch</a>. The German eyries ranged in height from 8 to 30&#160;m (26 to 98&#160;ft), averaging 20&#160;m (66&#160;ft), usually in tallest trees available.<sup id="cite_ref-Oehme_209-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oehme-209">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup> Nests can be on crags, whether or not trees may be available, depending on regional habitat.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_32-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> In 98 Norwegian eyries, only 8 were in trees, the rest were on cliffs. In comparison, against the 86% nests that were on cliffs in Norway, 79% known in Scotland were too, 77% in Iceland and all known Greenlandic nests were in cliffs or other rocky spots.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Willgohs_31-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willgohs-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ingólfsson_136-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ingólfsson-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Christensen_211-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Christensen-211">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup> Nest height on cliffs can be over 75&#160;m (246&#160;ft) above the nearest flat ground.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_9-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Rarely, they may nest on the ground or a low hummock, as well as smaller trees, low bushes, on sand banks or among reed beds.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_32-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Showing their adaptability, one pair even nested on a <a href="/wiki/Buoy" title="Buoy">buoy</a> on a Norwegian shipping route.<sup id="cite_ref-Willgohs_31-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willgohs-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Nests are usually huge, constructed of sticks and branches, averaging roughly 1&#160;m (3.3&#160;ft) across and up to 2&#160;m (6.6&#160;ft) deep, but can be several metres in width &amp; depth, lined variously with moss, greenery, seaweed or wool.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_9-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> The nest must have easy access for the eagles with a clear view of the surrounding environment, shelter from the elements and protection from nest predators. In forest of Finland, it has been estimated that perhaps only 1 in a thousand trees will prove attractive to white-tailed eagles.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Cliff nests often include lesser materials, some may be only a scooped shallow depression of soil, or pile of heather or juniper stems with stipes of dried <i><a href="/wiki/Laminaria" title="Laminaria">Laminaria</a></i>, but also sticks up to 1.5&#160;m (4.9&#160;ft) and several cm thick may be incorporated; almost any material available may line the nests such as seaweed, driftwood, even glass or metal floats and, in one to two cases, the dried skeleton a sheep.<sup id="cite_ref-Willgohs_31-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willgohs-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> In tree nests, the average nest diameter is about 1.5&#160;m (4.9&#160;ft), with nests in trees tending to be larger, more rigid and stronger. A relatively new 4 year old nest weighed 240&#160;kg (530&#160;lb) and one about twice that age can exceed 600&#160;kg (1,300&#160;lb) in mass. Some tree nests may range easily up to 2&#160;m (6.6&#160;ft) in diameter and 3&#160;m (9.8&#160;ft) deep after many repairs over years, with extreme sizes of perhaps another metre in diameter and depth, or “twice the height of a man”.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_32-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Willgohs_31-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willgohs-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-vonBlotzheim_88-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vonBlotzheim-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup> Tree nests may be lined with <a href="/wiki/Lichen" title="Lichen">lichen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moss" title="Moss">moss</a>, <a href="/wiki/Seaweed" title="Seaweed">seaweed</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fern" title="Fern">ferns</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grass" class="mw-redirect" title="Grass">grass</a>, <a href="/wiki/Woodrush" class="mw-redirect" title="Woodrush">woodrush</a>, <a href="/wiki/Calluna" title="Calluna">heather</a> or <i><a href="/wiki/Empetrum" title="Empetrum">Empetrum</a></i>; many tree nests may be lined with sheep's wool.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Willgohs_31-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willgohs-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Christensen_211-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Christensen-211">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup> The male of the pair more often brings nesting branches, while female takes the primary role in construction.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> A nest may be added to by the resident pair at as early as December onwards, but usually do so starting in March at high latitudes. A new nest may take several months to construct, but if an old nest is lost in late winter, pairs have been known to build a new nest faster than average in less than a month, however egg laying may be inhibited in such cases.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_32-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Repair to an existing nest can take about 18 days.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> White-tailed eagles have also used nests built by other species: <a href="/wiki/Black_kite" title="Black kite">black kite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Common_buzzard" title="Common buzzard">common buzzard</a>, <a href="/wiki/Common_raven" title="Common raven">common raven</a> (<i>Corvus corax</i>) and, subsequent to the eagles evicting them, <a href="/wiki/Osprey" title="Osprey">ospreys</a> and <a href="/wiki/Red_kite" title="Red kite">red kites</a>. <a href="/wiki/Eastern_imperial_eagle" title="Eastern imperial eagle">Eastern imperial eagles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saker_falcon" title="Saker falcon">saker</a> (<i>Falco cherrug</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Peregrine_falcon" title="Peregrine falcon">peregrine falcons</a> have used old white-tailed eagles nests, in the two swift falcon species they've been recorded in evicting pairs of white-tailed eagles from their eyries with persistent dive-bombing.<sup id="cite_ref-Fischer1_114-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fischer1-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-vonBlotzheim_88-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vonBlotzheim-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> In one case, after having been displaced by humans from an eyrie that had seen 30 years of white-tailed eagle use, an eagle pair returned to find the nest occupied by peregrine falcons. Despite many dogfights, the eagles constructed a nest higher up in the same tree.
