Although fighting continued, both species succeeded in raising 1 fledgling each.<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup> In Norway, pairs of ravens, peregrines and white-tailed eagles have been known to successfully nest on the same cliff face.<sup id="cite_ref-Willgohs_31-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willgohs-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Many small species of bird may nest in the immediate area of white-tailed eagle eyries, presumably due to incidental protection: <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_tree_sparrow" title="Eurasian tree sparrow">Eurasian tree sparrow</a> (<i>Passer montanus</i>), <a href="/wiki/White_wagtail" title="White wagtail">white wagtail</a> (<i>Motacilla alba</i>),  <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_treecreeper" title="Eurasian treecreeper">Eurasian treecreepers</a> (<i>Certhia familiaris</i>), <a href="/wiki/Common_redstart" title="Common redstart">common redstarts</a> (<i>Phoenicurus phoenicurus</i>), <a href="/wiki/European_crested_tit" title="European crested tit">European crested tits</a> (<i>Lophophanes cristatus</i>), <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_starling" class="mw-redirect" title="Eurasian starling">Eurasian starlings</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stock_dove" title="Stock dove">stock doves</a> (<i>Columba oenas</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Fischer1_114-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fischer1-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Fischer2_132-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fischer2-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> Like in the golden eagle, pairs of white-tailed eagles often build multiple nests on their home range over time and use them randomly over different years (sometimes using one for several consecutive years or changing nests every year over several years). The species may build from 1 to 11 nests, averaging 2.5 in Norway with pairs with up to 5 nests being not uncommon in that country.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_32-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Willgohs_31-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willgohs-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Eggs_and_incubation">Eggs and incubation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=White-tailed_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Eggs and incubation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Haliaeetus_albicilla_MWNH_0837.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Haliaeetus_albicilla_MWNH_0837.JPG/220px-Haliaeetus_albicilla_MWNH_0837.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="157" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Haliaeetus_albicilla_MWNH_0837.JPG/330px-Haliaeetus_albicilla_MWNH_0837.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Haliaeetus_albicilla_MWNH_0837.JPG/440px-Haliaeetus_albicilla_MWNH_0837.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3504" data-file-height="2508" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Haliaeetus_albicilla_MWNH_0837.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Egg, Collection <a href="/wiki/Museum_Wiesbaden" title="Museum Wiesbaden">Museum Wiesbaden</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Haliaeetus_albicilla_groenlandicus_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.96.3.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Haliaeetus_albicilla_groenlandicus_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.96.3.jpg/220px-Haliaeetus_albicilla_groenlandicus_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.96.3.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Haliaeetus_albicilla_groenlandicus_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.96.3.jpg/330px-Haliaeetus_albicilla_groenlandicus_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.96.3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Haliaeetus_albicilla_groenlandicus_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.96.3.jpg/440px-Haliaeetus_albicilla_groenlandicus_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.96.3.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2835" data-file-height="2835" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Haliaeetus_albicilla_groenlandicus_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.96.3.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>egg, <i>Haliaeetus albicilla groenlandicus</i> - <a href="/wiki/MHNT" class="mw-redirect" title="MHNT">MHNT</a></div></div></div>
<p>Individual white-tailed eagles tend to be remarkably consistent on egg-laying times from year to year, seemingly regardless of surrounding weather conditions.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Willgohs_31-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willgohs-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Egg laying does vary of course by latitude. Historic breeders from <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> (where they still usually breed) were reported to lay eggs in January, while in <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a> and the lower <a href="/wiki/Volga_river" class="mw-redirect" title="Volga river">Volga river</a> it is during February, in <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a> mostly during March and in the subarctic of <a href="/wiki/Lapland,_Finland" class="mw-redirect" title="Lapland, Finland">Lapland</a> and northern <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, to as late as late April to early May.<sup id="cite_ref-Bent_72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bent-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Flerov_71-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Flerov-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ingólfsson_136-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ingólfsson-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> White-tailed eagles in <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a> seem to have average egg laying about 3 weeks later, late March to early April, than those in coastal <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Willgohs_31-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willgohs-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bannerman_162-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bannerman-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> Even near Arctic in Finland, eggs were found as early as March 5 despite ranging often to as late as May.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Younger pairs may nest later on average than older ones.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_32-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> In white-tailed eagles, many (perhaps a majority) of clutches consist of 2 eggs, especially in Irish and Scottish nests, with 3 egg clutches being considered uncommon to rare there. 4 egg clutches have been recorded on the <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Skye" title="Isle of Skye">Isle of Skye</a>, as well as in at least <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a> and <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Willgohs_31-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willgohs-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Fischer2_132-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fischer2-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup> Clutch size in Scotland averages 1.56, while in Norway it is 2.16.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Willgohs_31-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willgohs-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> The eggs are a broad oval shape and dull white in colour, sometimes with a glossy sheen and are usually unmarked but for occasional yellowish stain.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_32-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Fresh egg weigh from 120 to 148&#160;g (4.2 to 5.2&#160;oz),  with an average of approximately 135&#160;g (4.8&#160;oz) for the 1st egg and 124&#160;g (4.4&#160;oz) for the 2nd. If replacement clutch is laid, usually a single egg is laid which may weigh as little as 112&#160;g (4.0&#160;oz).<sup id="cite_ref-vonBlotzheim_88-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vonBlotzheim-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bannerman_162-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bannerman-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Fentzloff_215-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fentzloff-215">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup> Average egg dimension seem to keep in accordance with the female body size, smallest mean size known were from <a href="/wiki/Turkey_(country)" class="mw-redirect" title="Turkey (country)">Turkey</a> where the average dimensions were 71.8&#160;mm ×&#160;56.2&#160;mm (2.83&#160;in ×&#160;2.21&#160;in) to the largest in 3 surveys from <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a>, where they were 78&#160;mm ×&#160;59.3&#160;mm (3.07&#160;in ×&#160;2.33&#160;in). In 54 eggs from Scotland they ranged from 67.5 to 84.2&#160;mm (2.66 to 3.31&#160;in), averaging 75.8&#160;mm (2.98&#160;in), in height by 53.4 to 64&#160;mm (2.10 to 2.52&#160;in), averaging 58.7&#160;mm (2.31&#160;in), in width.<sup id="cite_ref-Fischer1_114-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fischer1-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bent_72-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bent-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> In comparison, 90 eggs from mainland Europe ranged from 66 to 88.2&#160;mm (2.60 to 3.47&#160;in), averaging 73.4&#160;mm (2.89&#160;in), by 54 to 63.5&#160;mm (2.13 to 2.50&#160;in), averaging 57.6&#160;mm (2.27&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_32-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Eggs may be compromised quickly by frost and snow in relatively early clutches.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_32-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Incubation starts as soon as the 1st egg is laid. In the species, a 2 to 3 day interval between the first and 2nd egg being laid (as well as hatching) is recorded.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brown_32-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> The female does up to 80-90% of incubation and all known nighttime incubating.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_32-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> However, in Norway, the male of a pair could contribute up to about 27% of incubation to daylight hours. There may be as many as 11 changes of the incubating individual within a day. The longest period recorded the clutch was unguarded was 20 minutes, although 1 pair perched nearby the nest without incubating for 48 minutes. The clutches in Norway left alone only 2-4% of the time.<sup id="cite_ref-Willgohs_31-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willgohs-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> In some cases female may do all the incubation alone, however.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_32-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> When the female is off the nest, she sometimes kills prey and returns with a full crop, but prey capture during incubation is often up to the male. Late into incubation, shift changes decreases and the female will sit tight and will crouch down at the approach of a human rather than leave the nest. Calling and displaying are reduced during incubation, but occasionally both may leave the clutch and display still.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_32-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> The time range for incubation can vary from 34 to 46 days, but usually are between 38 and 42 days. A calculated mean time range for incubation of 38.3 days.<sup id="cite_ref-Fischer1_114-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fischer1-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Fischer2_132-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fischer2-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Hatching.2C_development_and_fledgling"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Hatching,_development_and_fledgling">Hatching, development and fledgling</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=White-tailed_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Hatching, development and fledgling">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Haliaeetus_albicilla_070525_njg.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Haliaeetus_albicilla_070525_njg.JPG/220px-Haliaeetus_albicilla_070525_njg.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Haliaeetus_albicilla_070525_njg.JPG/330px-Haliaeetus_albicilla_070525_njg.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Haliaeetus_albicilla_070525_njg.JPG/440px-Haliaeetus_albicilla_070525_njg.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Haliaeetus_albicilla_070525_njg.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A large nestling in <a href="/wiki/Brandenburg" title="Brandenburg">Brandenburg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>In 1983, it was listed that brood size is one 56.5% of the time and two 40.8% of the time in 8 studies from different parts of the range; only 2.5% of these studies had a brood size of three and only 1 from Norway was a brood size of four recorded. From 14 studies, the mean number of chicks in a brood may range from 1.1 in western <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a> to 1.9 in southern <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a>, with average overall brood size of 1.52. However, these datasets may be skewered lower than natural brood size as a majority these are from the mid to late 20th century when pesticide use considerably lowered the mean number of hatchlings in many parts of the range.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Helander_2-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Helander-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Willgohs_31-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willgohs-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-vonBlotzheim_88-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vonBlotzheim-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> New hatchlings will weigh about 90 to 100&#160;g (3.2 to 3.5&#160;oz) but in Hokkaido new hatchlings were surprisingly notably heavier at 110 to 115&#160;g (3.9 to 4.1&#160;oz).<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_32-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mori_126-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mori-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> The sex of nestlings can be identified using field methods, or using <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHelanderHailerVilà2007_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHelanderHailerVilà2007-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Initially, the hatchlings have a creamy white down which is longest and whitest on the head and often dirty grayish on wings and rump. Before they start walking about the nest, their underside may have several bare patches.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> The nestlings first become audible at around 2 to 3 days and have become active enough to move around the nest and excrete over the nest edge by 10 days old.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_32-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> The initial down is replaced by a thick woolly coat of longer, coarser grayish down, which is usually darker on the crown, underparts and flanks. The legs and cere at this young nestling age can range from pinkish to pale yellow.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> By about 30 days the first feathers poke through the down.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> The eaglets can feed themselves starting at 35–40 days.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_32-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> At 6 weeks (40 days) they are more firm on their feet and between this and the following week feathers take over the down, with patches of down remaining but usually gone by week 7.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Wing flapping begins only when the wings are partially feathered at 42 days.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_32-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Around seven weeks of age, the eaglet is more alert and stronger and frequently manipulates sticks and walks more so.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> At 8 weeks only the long feathers of the wing and tail have yet to develop fully and eaglets tend to start exploring the surrounding branches.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brown_32-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> The eaglets will attempt their first flight at about 70 days and will usually be flying well by about 90 days of age.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_32-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mori_126-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mori-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> Fledgling occurs around mid- to late July in Norway, and about 3–4 weeks later in Russian White Sea.<sup id="cite_ref-Willgohs_31-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willgohs-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Flerov_71-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Flerov-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Seeadler_684-2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Seeadler_684-2.jpg/220px-Seeadler_684-2.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="181" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Seeadler_684-2.jpg/330px-Seeadler_684-2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Seeadler_684-2.jpg/440px-Seeadler_684-2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="777" data-file-height="641" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Seeadler_684-2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Young white-tailed eagles quickly become assured fish predators.</div></div></div>
<p>The female of the white-tailed eagle pair seems to do all the brooding early on and will be especially reluctant to leave the nest as well.<sup id="cite_ref-Willgohs_31-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willgohs-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Fischer2_132-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fischer2-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> Thereafter, at 14 to 28 days, brooding behaviour by the female gradually declines.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_32-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> The male may start to brood occasionally around this time period but will not do so at night.<sup id="cite_ref-Willgohs_31-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willgohs-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Fischer2_132-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fischer2-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup>  Females may sit on the nest or shelter the fledglings from rain even 28 days after fledging but usually such behavior is much reduced.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_32-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Chicks may be fed as many as 11 times in 24 hours by the female who usually dismantles prey brought by the male.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Willgohs_31-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willgohs-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> To 28 days, male continues to do most of the prey capture but thereafter female does much of it and both parents begin to leave kills on the nest for the eaglets to consume.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_32-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> As the chicks grow older, favored food often switches from fish to birds to meet their increasing food requirements as well as prey behavior as waterfowl may be flightless during <a href="/wiki/Plumage" title="Plumage">eclipse</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Willgohs_31-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willgohs-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hoarding_(animal_behavior)" title="Hoarding (animal behavior)">Caches</a> are often depleted quickly late into nestling development.<sup id="cite_ref-Willgohs_31-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willgohs-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> At start of fledgling, 4-5 carcasses may be brought in a week but by the end only 1 to 2 are usually brought, probably to encourage the young to start their own prey captures.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_32-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> After leaving the nest, the young usually stay nearby for another 35–40 days and may still be largely fed by their parents but gradually learn to take their own prey.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_32-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mori_126-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mori-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> In continental Europe, the young eagles are gone from the nest as early as early July to about August 10 and fully independent by late August. In this late summer stage, they may learn quickly to feed on stranded fish or to capture ducks flightless in eclipse. Juvenile eagles may remain for a long time on their parents range and apparently are not resented or repelled even to the ages of 1 to 3 years. However, usually by their first winter they will have congregated with other unrelated juveniles.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brown_32-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Communal juvenile roosts in Norway can maintain 30-40 white-tailed eagles usually in trees or steep slopes of offshore islands.<sup id="cite_ref-Willgohs_31-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willgohs-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Sexual maturity is reached at 5 to 6 years of age.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Willgohs_31-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willgohs-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nesting_failures_and_longevity">Nesting failures and longevity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=White-tailed_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Nesting failures and longevity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:An_adult_and_a_juvenile_white-tailed_eagle_(Haliaeetus_albicilla)_fighting.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/An_adult_and_a_juvenile_white-tailed_eagle_%28Haliaeetus_albicilla%29_fighting.jpg/220px-An_adult_and_a_juvenile_white-tailed_eagle_%28Haliaeetus_albicilla%29_fighting.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="124" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/An_adult_and_a_juvenile_white-tailed_eagle_%28Haliaeetus_albicilla%29_fighting.jpg/330px-An_adult_and_a_juvenile_white-tailed_eagle_%28Haliaeetus_albicilla%29_fighting.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/An_adult_and_a_juvenile_white-tailed_eagle_%28Haliaeetus_albicilla%29_fighting.jpg/440px-An_adult_and_a_juvenile_white-tailed_eagle_%28Haliaeetus_albicilla%29_fighting.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5342" data-file-height="3005" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:An_adult_and_a_juvenile_white-tailed_eagle_(Haliaeetus_albicilla)_fighting.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An adult and a juvenile white-tailed eagle fighting.</div></div></div>
<p>Normally white-tailed eagles succeed in raising 1-2 from a clutch of 2 and 2 from a clutch of 3.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_32-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> The average breeding success seems to be about 1.1-1.6 young per annum (i.e. 45-48 young from 40 observed cases, with 16 failures) (1.6 young per pair in 93 recorded Norwegian nests).<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_32-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Willgohs_31-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willgohs-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> About 33% of nesting attempts will fail to produce any young, and this is sometimes as many as 75% (at times of heavy persecution or pesticide use).<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Beyond manmade threats to white-tailed eagle nestlings, starvation and nest collapse are considerable causes of nestling deaths.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brown_32-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Natural threats in the nest may also include <a href="/wiki/Siblicide" title="Siblicide">siblicide</a> (or “cainism”) wherein the largest nestling behave aggressively and gradually kill their smaller sibling, which is often consumed.<sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup> Despite claims to contrary,<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_32-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> siblicide in white-tailed eagle does occur with some regularity in some parts of the range, from Germany to Hokkaido although is certainly less frequent than in some eagle species, including the <a href="/wiki/Golden_eagle" title="Golden eagle">golden eagle</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mori_126-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mori-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup> Single cases were reported in the <a href="/wiki/White_Sea" title="White Sea">White Sea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a>, and it may occur situationally as in many birds of prey when prey populations are low or prey capture is inhibited by poor weather.<sup id="cite_ref-Flerov_71-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Flerov-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup> Siblicide can be avoided in white-tailed eagles with manipulative techniques, which may include taking out and hand-rearing the runt, put a chick into a nest with slightly younger brood which it is about the same size as, or taking the first egg out and putting it back in later so it develops evenly with its younger sibling. Surplus chicks are sometimes removed from nests to use in <a href="/wiki/Reintroduction" class="mw-redirect" title="Reintroduction">reintroduction</a> programs in areas where the species has died out. If left in the nest, they often die sooner or later, as with most large eagles. In such programs the birds are raised in boxes on platforms in the tree canopy and fed in such a way that they cannot see the human supplying their food, until they are old enough to fly and thus find their own food.<sup id="cite_ref-Helander2_205-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Helander2-205">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Meyburg_219-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meyburg-219">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Known natural predators of white-tailed eagle eggs and nestlings include <a href="/wiki/Red_fox" title="Red fox">red foxes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marten" title="Marten">martens</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bear" title="Bear">bears</a>, especially if nesting in overly accessible rock formations, while <a href="/wiki/Wild_boar" title="Wild boar">wild boars</a> have been recorded eating eggs and nestlings that’ve fallen out of the nest too early. Avian predation, overall a seemingly rare occurrence, of white-tailed eagle eggs and nestlings have been reported as <a href="/wiki/Crow" title="Crow">crows</a>, <a href="/wiki/Common_raven" title="Common raven">common ravens</a> and <a href="/wiki/Western_marsh_harrier" title="Western marsh harrier">western marsh harriers</a>, which are likely to succeed in cases where nest attendance is low or if successful in driving away the parents via fierce mobbing.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Flerov_71-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Flerov-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Fischer2_132-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fischer2-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> Furthermore, Eurasian eagle owls have been reported to prey upon "fairly large" nestlings of white-tailed eagle in nighttime ambushes.<sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola_184-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola-184">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> Many juveniles do not survive their first year post-independence. In the 1970s, an average of 56% banded first-year white-tailed eagles in Norway, Sweden and Greenland were found dead, most often having been shot.<sup id="cite_ref-Hagen,Y._1976_86-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hagen,Y._1976-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Helander3_220-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Helander3-220">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup> In eastern Germany, of 194 white-tailed eagles found dead between 1946 and 1972, of those where cause of death could be determined, 39% had been shot, accidents (especially powerlines) accounted for 6%, territorial disputes 7.5% and conservatively 13% were from poisoning.<sup id="cite_ref-Oehme_209-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oehme-209">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup> It is estimated that the survival of those that reach adulthood at 70%.<sup id="cite_ref-Cramp_38-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cramp-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> Another estimate of average lifespan for those who reach adulthood is just over 12 years.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_32-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Widely reported individuals have been found to live for 25 years or more,<sup id="cite_ref-mtv3_222-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mtv3-222">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup> Another estimate of mean lifespan (presumably without persecution) is 21 years.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThe_Nature_of_Scotland49_17-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThe_Nature_of_Scotland49-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> At one point, the oldest banded white-tailed eagle known in the wild known lived for 27 years. However, a wild eagle of the species was subsequently recorded to life for 33.1 years. White-tailed eagles in captivity have lived for more than 40 years, though their exactly captive longevity record is not known.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Helander3_220-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Helander3-220">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Relationship_with_humans">Relationship with humans</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=White-tailed_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Relationship with humans">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Haliaeetus_albicilla_-_Museum_fur_Naturkunde,_Berlin_-_DSC00208.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Haliaeetus_albicilla_-_Museum_fur_Naturkunde%2C_Berlin_-_DSC00208.JPG/220px-Haliaeetus_albicilla_-_Museum_fur_Naturkunde%2C_Berlin_-_DSC00208.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="330" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Haliaeetus_albicilla_-_Museum_fur_Naturkunde%2C_Berlin_-_DSC00208.JPG/330px-Haliaeetus_albicilla_-_Museum_fur_Naturkunde%2C_Berlin_-_DSC00208.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Haliaeetus_albicilla_-_Museum_fur_Naturkunde%2C_Berlin_-_DSC00208.JPG/440px-Haliaeetus_albicilla_-_Museum_fur_Naturkunde%2C_Berlin_-_DSC00208.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3648" data-file-height="5472" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Haliaeetus_albicilla_-_Museum_fur_Naturkunde,_Berlin_-_DSC00208.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>White-tailed eagle skeleton.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Extirpations">Extirpations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=White-tailed_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Extirpations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The white-tailed eagle formerly bred over much wider area, extending west to much of <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">western Europe</a> and perhaps south almost continuously in that region to the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean" class="mw-redirect" title="Mediterranean">Mediterranean</a>. From the 19th century, the species underwent a huge, well-documented decline. Ultimately the white-tailed eagle was almost extinct in Europe, extirpated from all but <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavia</a> (mainly remaining in <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a>) and some sparse patches of <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Europe" title="Eastern Europe">eastern Europe</a>. They were extinct in the entire <a href="/wiki/British_Isles" title="British Isles">British Isles</a> by the early 1900s.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> At one time, the white-tailed eagle bred down to <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> in <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>, particularly around <a href="/wiki/Lake_Manzala" title="Lake Manzala">Lake Manzala</a> with individuals wandering rarely to <a href="/wiki/Algeria" title="Algeria">Algeria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tunisia" title="Tunisia">Tunisia</a>. It is likely that habitat degradation and drying conditions caused the extirpation of the species as all but a vagrant in Egypt.<sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224">&#91;224&#93;</a></sup> Two pairs that nested in the Jordan valley of <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a> ceased to breed, apparently due to agricultural chemicals, in the early 1950s.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> The species also once bred in northern <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a> but is not found reliably there even in winter in modern times.<sup id="cite_ref-Cramp_38-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cramp-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Causes_of_decline">Causes of decline</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=White-tailed_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Causes of decline">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In Britain, the opinion towards white-tailed eagles became negative in sync with the creation of farmland and commercial fishing, as it was quickly perceived that they were competitors for resources and could deplete the livelihood of flocks for shepherds (despite this being largely untrue) and game animals for gamekeepers. Therefore, laws were passed to facilitate their destruction.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225">&#91;225&#93;</a></sup> Already by the end of the 18th century, down from breeding in all appropriate habitat, the English population was down to only localized breeding, namely in the <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Wight" title="Isle of Wight">Isle of Wight</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lundy" title="Lundy">Lundy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Man" title="Isle of Man">Isle of Man</a> and (probably) near <a href="/wiki/Plymouth" title="Plymouth">Plymouth</a>; within a couple decades the species only remained in the <a href="/wiki/Lake_District" title="Lake District">Lake District</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Bannerman_162-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bannerman-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> Before the advent of firearms, few people in England and Scotland were highly motivated to kill eagles since this could be time-consuming and hazardous process, therefore the British government rose the bounty on eagles to a very high 5 shillings a head by the turn of the 18th century.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Unfortunately, eyries in many coastal sites  were found to be easily accessible so that destroying or selling eggs was common. Subsequent to systematic persecution, in Greenland 62% of eyries found to be "easily accessible" and only 13% foiled all attempts to reach them. Similar findings were found in sea cliff nests in Iceland, Norway and Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Willgohs_31-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willgohs-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Christensen_211-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Christensen-211">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup> White-tailed eagles are more vulnerable to direct persecution than golden eagles since most nests are highly accessible for white-tailed eagle but not for golden eagles which usually nest in mountainous, precipitously rocky terrain, in contrast to sea cliff nests of which 67-87% were found to be accessible.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ingólfsson_136-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ingólfsson-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Christensen_211-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Christensen-211">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup> Before firearms were widely available in Scotland and Norway automatic traps were utilized wherein carrion was laid out to entice an eagle with a person hiding in a near subterranean trap waited until the eagle was distracted, at that point grabbing the eagle by the leg. Petrified by the darkness once drug below, white-tailed eagles apparently offer no resistance once caught. However, habitat had to be favorable and even when conditions were correct, success at capture as such was low.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Willgohs_31-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willgohs-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bannerman_162-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bannerman-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> The main driver of declines before firearms and industrialized poisons was habitat alterations.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> After about the 1840s, firearms became available and declines accelerated considerably, by 1916 the last nesting pair in all of Britain attempted to raise a brood on the <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Skye" title="Isle of Skye">isle of Skye</a>. While other ecological factors have been considered in this decline, stringent research has shown the extirpation here was fully correlated to intentional, rapacious predation by man.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bannerman_162-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bannerman-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> Many <a href="/wiki/Gamekeeper" class="mw-redirect" title="Gamekeeper">gamekeepers</a> poisoned and shot eagles and destroyed nearly any nest they encountered. A few more enlightened landowners forbade the killing of eagles but there's evidence that the gamekeepers sometimes chose to destroy eagles regardless of the rule of law. On deer forest, eagles were tolerated later than in other British areas, but destructions accelerated there by the late 1800s.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Also many white-tailed eagles were poisoned by shepherds who considered it enemy of the flock.<sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">&#91;226&#93;</a></sup> Elsewhere in Europe, persecution rates in the 19th and 20th century were just as drastic. In <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a>, more than 400 white-tailed eagles were killed in 2 decades by a single hunter.<sup id="cite_ref-Bijleveld_67-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bijleveld-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> In Norway between 1959 and 1968, an average of 169 eagles were killed annually; with a maximum of 221 in 1961.<sup id="cite_ref-Willgohs_31-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willgohs-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Around the year 1860, an author estimated that about 400 were being killed annually throughout Germany.<sup id="cite_ref-Bijleveld_67-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bijleveld-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Between 1946 and 1972 in eastern Germany, a total of 194 dead white-tailed eagles were found, about half of them shot, after governmental protection of the species had been instituted there.<sup id="cite_ref-Oehme_209-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oehme-209">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Top predators, especially those that are aquatic and coastal, are almost immediately vulnerable upon exposure to <a href="/wiki/DDT" title="DDT">DDT</a>. Therefore, white-tailed eagles are highly susceptible to this pesticide, as are similar fish eaters, such as <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_otter" title="Eurasian otter">otters</a>, and bird eaters, such as <a href="/wiki/Peregrine_falcon" title="Peregrine falcon">peregrine falcons</a>. Distributed by man nearly across the developed world as an insecticide in the 1950s, by the early 1970s, authors found many species of bird experienced reduced egg shell thickness. Thus the incubating parents inadvertently crushed their normally hardy eggs and, in turn, many water birds and raptors had their nesting success dropped precipitously.<sup id="cite_ref-Newton_227-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Newton-227">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup> In fact, the species was found to have the highest concentration of DDT of any European raptor.<sup id="cite_ref-228" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-228">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup> Egg shell thickness was found down from 0.62&#160;mm (0.024&#160;in) prior to 1935 from 1969 to 1975 down to only 0.52&#160;mm (0.020&#160;in), a 16% reduction.<sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup> In Sweden, coastal birds were considerably more effected by DDT than the inland birds of <a href="/wiki/Lapland,_Sweden" class="mw-redirect" title="Lapland, Sweden">Lapland</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Helander3_220-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Helander3-220">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup> In eastern Germany, where pesticide use was heavy, only 1 out of 28 nesting attempts were known to succeed in 1976.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Overall, about 75% nesting attempts failed in western Germany, Finland and the Swedish Baltic area.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Other environmental pollutants affecting the species include heavy metals which affect individuals through <a href="/wiki/Bioaccumulation" title="Bioaccumulation">bioaccumulation</a>. The amount of white-tailed eagles killed by mercury poisoning rose from 6.4% during 1946-1957 to 24.6% in 1958–1965 in Germany.<sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231">&#91;231&#93;</a></sup> It was estimated that pesticides and metal contaminations reduced the white-tailed eagle population in Hungary from 1957 to 1967 by about 50-60%.<sup id="cite_ref-Bijleveld_67-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bijleveld-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Lead poisoning, caused by lead bullets left in carcasses that the white-tailed eagles will eat in winter, is also another issue faced by the species. Fatal and near fatal levels of lead exposure continues to be a major issue in the 21st century in many parts of the range, at least from <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a> to <a href="/wiki/Hokkaido" title="Hokkaido">Hokkaido</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233">&#91;233&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-234" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-234">&#91;234&#93;</a></sup> Despite regulations on their usage, lead and mercury poisonings were found to be the cause of death of 61 white-tailed eagles found in Germany from 1993 to 2000.<sup id="cite_ref-235" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-235">&#91;235&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Conservation_measures">Conservation measures</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=White-tailed_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Conservation measures">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Haliaeetus_albicilla_2_(Martin_Mecnarowski).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Haliaeetus_albicilla_2_%28Martin_Mecnarowski%29.jpg/220px-Haliaeetus_albicilla_2_%28Martin_Mecnarowski%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="290" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Haliaeetus_albicilla_2_%28Martin_Mecnarowski%29.jpg/330px-Haliaeetus_albicilla_2_%28Martin_Mecnarowski%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Haliaeetus_albicilla_2_%28Martin_Mecnarowski%29.jpg/440px-Haliaeetus_albicilla_2_%28Martin_Mecnarowski%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="607" data-file-height="800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Haliaeetus_albicilla_2_(Martin_Mecnarowski).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A dark-hued juvenile feeding on the carcass of a large fish, likely that of a large <a href="/wiki/Common_carp" title="Common carp">carp</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>In order to offset the numerous chemical and metal based poisoning that humans were inadvertently exposing the species to, a widespread operation was undertaken to feed white-tailed eagles uncontaminated foods in Sweden. Here, carcasses from slaughterhouses placed in areas free of human disturbance, usually fields, bogs, marshes or frozen lakes, from October to March (after these months, the eagles will ignore carrion in favor of capture of live prey). Apparently, breeding success improved from 29% to 44% when the program began. In southern Sweden subsequent to the feedings, 5 of 11 breeding pairs were successful and 2 previously unoccupied territories were taken over by new pairs, thus winter feeding was seemingly highly beneficial to local eagles.<sup id="cite_ref-Helander3_220-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Helander3-220">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236">&#91;236&#93;</a></sup> Similar winter feeding stations set up in Finland from 1972 to 1978.<sup id="cite_ref-237" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-237">&#91;237&#93;</a></sup> In Sweden, brood size has varied from 1.3 per nest prior to 1950, down to 0.3 in 1965–1985. Now the brood sizes have increased, at somewhat less than 1 brood size on average, but still somewhat less productivity than historical numbers.<sup id="cite_ref-238" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-238">&#91;238&#93;</a></sup> In several parts of the European range, especially southern <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Central_Europe" title="Central Europe">central Europe</a>, protections have allowed white-tailed eagles to recolonize former parts of their range.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Since recolonizing <a href="/wiki/Schleswig-Holstein" title="Schleswig-Holstein">Schleswig-Holstein</a>, <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a> in 1947, numbers have increased slowly; from 1975–2008, increases were recorded at 6.7% in population per annum and enjoyed much higher productivity.<sup id="cite_ref-Krüger_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Krüger-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Perhaps particularly key has been conserving white-tailed eagle habitats. In the 1970s, 75% of the potential and current white-tailed eagle territories became protected in Schleswig-Holstein.<sup id="cite_ref-Bijleveld_67-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bijleveld-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> In Poland, 78% of forests are state owned and clusters of 10 or more trees are preserved around each white-tailed eagle eyrie.<sup id="cite_ref-Bogucki_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bogucki-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> The Swedish Forest Service limits logging activities to within 200&#160;m (660&#160;ft) of a nest tree which expands to within 1,000&#160;m (3,300&#160;ft) during breeding season; however this acts not as a law but only as an advisory where the eyries are located on private land.<sup id="cite_ref-Helander3_220-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Helander3-220">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup> Limits on recreational activity were established as these can disturb white-tailed eagle nests especially in well-trodden countries such as Germany and Sweden, but even in the remote Russian <a href="/wiki/Kandalaksha" title="Kandalaksha">Kandalaksha</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Flerov_71-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Flerov-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> In northern <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a>, artificial eyries were constructed for their use. Out of 19, 14 were visited by white-tailed eagle, eggs laid in at least 9 and 2 ultimately fledged young.<sup id="cite_ref-Stjernberg_62-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stjernberg-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Regulations have been established on direct use of pesticides that harm white-tailed eagles such as DDT, however because of political variances, despite widespread monitoring, not at all areas are strictly protected.<sup id="cite_ref-Movalli_239-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Movalli-239">&#91;239&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-240" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-240">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup> Similarly, regulations on lead usage in hunting has been inconsistent in comparison with the stronger efforts to ban lead bullet fragments in <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-241" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-241">&#91;241&#93;</a></sup> The residuals of mercury from various fungicides (now banned), air particles and pollution run-off water have caused huge concentrations in fish of many different areas, which continues to effect humans as well as entire ecosystems of wildlife. Unfortunately, methylmercury is still difficult to manage in Europe as it is elsewhere.<sup id="cite_ref-Newton_227-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Newton-227">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Movalli_239-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Movalli-239">&#91;239&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-242" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-242">&#91;242&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reintroduction">Reintroduction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=White-tailed_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Reintroduction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Haliaeetus_albicilla_-Loch_Portree,_Isle_of_Skye,_Scotland_-flying-8.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Haliaeetus_albicilla_-Loch_Portree%2C_Isle_of_Skye%2C_Scotland_-flying-8.jpg/220px-Haliaeetus_albicilla_-Loch_Portree%2C_Isle_of_Skye%2C_Scotland_-flying-8.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Haliaeetus_albicilla_-Loch_Portree%2C_Isle_of_Skye%2C_Scotland_-flying-8.jpg/330px-Haliaeetus_albicilla_-Loch_Portree%2C_Isle_of_Skye%2C_Scotland_-flying-8.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Haliaeetus_albicilla_-Loch_Portree%2C_Isle_of_Skye%2C_Scotland_-flying-8.jpg/440px-Haliaeetus_albicilla_-Loch_Portree%2C_Isle_of_Skye%2C_Scotland_-flying-8.jpg 2x" data-file-width="960" data-file-height="1280" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Haliaeetus_albicilla_-Loch_Portree,_Isle_of_Skye,_Scotland_-flying-8.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Adult in flight in <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Skye" title="Isle of Skye">Isle of Skye</a>, Scotland, from the population of reintroduced birds of Norwegian stock.</div></div></div>
<p>The first attempts at <a href="/wiki/Reintroduction" class="mw-redirect" title="Reintroduction">reintroduction</a> in Scotland were in 1959 in <a href="/wiki/Glen_Etive" title="Glen Etive">Glen Etive</a>, <a href="/wiki/Argyll" title="Argyll">Argyll</a> abortively, followed by a better informed but also ultimately unsuccessful attempt on <a href="/wiki/Fair_Isle" title="Fair Isle">Fair Isle</a> in 1968.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Successful reintroduction into Scotland did not occur until the 1970s, with the isle of <a href="/wiki/R%C3%B9m" title="Rùm">Rùm</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Inner_Hebrides" title="Inner Hebrides">inner Hebrides</a> being chosen because of its large size (10,600&#160;ha (26,000 acres)) with access to the <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Skye" title="Isle of Skye">isle of Skye</a> (where last native pair known in Britain last bred in 1916) and it is only 24&#160;km (15&#160;mi) from the mainland. Also Rùm hosts large seabird colonies that make for viable prey, including <a href="/wiki/Common_eider" title="Common eider">eider</a> (<i>Somateria mollissima</i>), <a href="/wiki/European_shag" title="European shag">shag</a> (<i>Phalacrocorax aristotelis</i>), <a href="/wiki/Auk" title="Auk">auks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gull" title="Gull">gulls</a>; as well as one of the few in Britain of <a href="/wiki/Manx_shearwater" title="Manx shearwater">manx shearwater</a> (<i>Puffinus puffinus</i>). Furthermore, mainly as a source of carrion, were a population of around 1500 <a href="/wiki/Red_deer" title="Red deer">red deer</a> and 200 feral <a href="/wiki/Goat" title="Goat">goats</a>; otters and gulls were also numerous and available to <a href="/wiki/Kleptoparasitism" title="Kleptoparasitism">kleptoparasitize</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Love2_11-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love2-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> The birds to be used for reintroduction were gathered as nestlings from western <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a>, as this is the nearest native breeding population. The young eagles were either kept in high grade fowl cages or tethered all within reach of an artificial eyrie, cover and feeding stations. Direct human contact, which the naturally wild young eagles tended to shun anyway, is minimal short of veterinary care. Releases were carried by taking bird out tethered, wearing a leather hood to prevent <a href="/wiki/Imprinting_(psychology)" title="Imprinting (psychology)">imprinting</a> and then releasing with a radio-monitor. Most of the eagles, despite no direct parenting, turned out to be competent hunters within a couple of weeks or do well at stealing meals, including from other released eagles. Despite some dying before release due to illness and some found dead subsequent to release, most survived. A total of 95 birds were received for the Rùm reintroductions and 82 were successfully released between 1975 and 1987.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Love2_11-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love2-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> The white-tailed eagle now breeds throughout the <a href="/wiki/Outer_Hebrides" title="Outer Hebrides">Western Isles</a> and the mainland coast of <a href="/wiki/Wester_Ross" title="Wester Ross">Wester Ross</a>. However, a low reproductive output of reintroduced Scottish eagles was recorded in 1996, and it was advocated that additional releases were needed.<sup id="cite_ref-243" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-243">&#91;243&#93;</a></sup> In August 2008, an additional fifteen chicks raised in Norway were released at a secret location in <a href="/wiki/Fife" title="Fife">Fife</a>, in expectation of reintroducing the species to the east coast of Scotland as well.<sup id="cite_ref-bbc_244-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-244">&#91;244&#93;</a></sup> Breeding success of reintroduced birds in Scotland (from 1975-1985 &amp; 1993-1998) is moderate compared overall in species, in 1982-1992: productivity was 0.38, with a mean fledgling number 1.61. In comparison, for the years 1993–2000, productivity was 0.61 and fledgling number was 1.48, while in 2000–2007, productivity was 0.7 and mean fledgling number 1.44.<sup id="cite_ref-Evans2_245-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Evans2-245">&#91;245&#93;</a></sup> Overall in Britain, there are estimated 36 breeding pairs in 2006 and 40 in 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThe_Nature_of_Scotland49_17-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThe_Nature_of_Scotland49-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESnowPerrins199848_246-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESnowPerrins199848-246">&#91;246&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-rspb2_247-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rspb2-247">&#91;247&#93;</a></sup> Juvenile survival rates are somewhat low overall compared to other areas.<sup id="cite_ref-Evans2_245-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Evans2-245">&#91;245&#93;</a></sup> Reintroduction efforts succeeded in the <a href="/wiki/Bohemia" title="Bohemia">Bohemia</a> area of the <a href="/wiki/Czech_Republic" title="Czech Republic">Czech Republic</a> as well, where the biologists similarly followed the guidelines of guarding of occupied eyries and provision of safe foods.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_9-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-248" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-248">&#91;248&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-249" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-249">&#91;249&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The white-tailed eagle is also being reintroduced to <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a>, where its <a href="/wiki/Irish_language" title="Irish language">Irish</a> name of Iolar Mara (sea eagle) reflects its historic association with the island's long coast. The Irish program was begun in the summer of 2007. Fifteen to twenty young eagles from <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a> are being released each spring into the <a href="/wiki/Killarney_National_Park" title="Killarney National Park">Killarney National Park</a> in the south-west of Ireland. This comprehensive project will last a number of years, with many more eagles being released. The species has a rich history on the island but became extinct in Ireland in the 1900s due to persecution from landowners.<sup id="cite_ref-rte_250-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rte-250">&#91;250&#93;</a></sup> The last pair bred on the coast of Mayo in 1912.<sup id="cite_ref-birdwatchireland_251-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-birdwatchireland-251">&#91;251&#93;</a></sup> In 2007, a hundred local sheep farmers gathered at Kerry airport to protest the eagles' arrival. Irish Farming Association Hill Committee chairman Mr O'Leary said he had no doubt the eagles would take lambs.<sup id="cite_ref-Drimnagh_252-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Drimnagh-252">&#91;252&#93;</a></sup> Since their reintroduction seven eagles have been confirmed poisoned in County Kerry, two suspected of having been poisoned, and one shot. A 13th eagle released in Kerry was shot in <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a>. Twenty more eagles were due for release in 2010. However, Dr Allan Mee, in charge of the sea eagle project, stated "the continuing loss of eagles to poisoning had cast a shadow over the future of the ambitious programme."<sup id="cite_ref-irishtimes_253-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-irishtimes-253">&#91;253&#93;</a></sup> The first white-tailed eagle breeding pair since 1912 nested 100 years later on <a href="/wiki/Lough_Derg_(Shannon)" title="Lough Derg (Shannon)">Lough Derg</a> (Loch Deirgeirt), marking a great success for the Irish reintroduction programme.<sup id="cite_ref-irishtimes2_254-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-irishtimes2-254">&#91;254&#93;</a></sup> In early May 2013, the first eaglets were born in Ireland since the re-introduction programme began; one in the Killarney National Park and two in County Clare.<sup id="cite_ref-rte2_255-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rte2-255">&#91;255&#93;</a></sup> In Spring 2015, five nests hatched chicks in 4 counties in Ireland - Clare, Cork, Galway and Kerry.<sup id="cite_ref-irishtimes3_256-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-irishtimes3-256">&#91;256&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2019, a reintroduction project on the <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Wight" title="Isle of Wight">Isle of Wight</a> was approved by the British government. It is hoped that establishing a breeding population in the coastal county will lead to the species re-colonising the South Coast.<sup id="cite_ref-257" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-257">&#91;257&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Current_status">Current status</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=White-tailed_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Current status">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sz%C3%A9ler%C5%91m%C5%B1vek_hat%C3%A1sa_a_mad%C3%A1rvil%C3%A1gra.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Sz%C3%A9ler%C5%91m%C5%B1vek_hat%C3%A1sa_a_mad%C3%A1rvil%C3%A1gra.jpg/220px-Sz%C3%A9ler%C5%91m%C5%B1vek_hat%C3%A1sa_a_mad%C3%A1rvil%C3%A1gra.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="173" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Sz%C3%A9ler%C5%91m%C5%B1vek_hat%C3%A1sa_a_mad%C3%A1rvil%C3%A1gra.jpg/330px-Sz%C3%A9ler%C5%91m%C5%B1vek_hat%C3%A1sa_a_mad%C3%A1rvil%C3%A1gra.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c1/Sz%C3%A9ler%C5%91m%C5%B1vek_hat%C3%A1sa_a_mad%C3%A1rvil%C3%A1gra.jpg 2x" data-file-width="339" data-file-height="267" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sz%C3%A9ler%C5%91m%C5%B1vek_hat%C3%A1sa_a_mad%C3%A1rvil%C3%A1gra.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A white-tailed eagle killed by a wind turbine.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:White_tailed_Eagle_from_Bridgwater_Bay.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/da/White_tailed_Eagle_from_Bridgwater_Bay.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="383" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="200" data-file-height="383" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:White_tailed_Eagle_from_Bridgwater_Bay.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A white tailed eagle was shot in the winter of 1857 at Stolford in <a href="/wiki/Bridgwater_Bay" title="Bridgwater Bay">Bridgwater Bay</a> and subsequently preserved for display.  It may be seen at the Somerset Heritage Centre (TA2 6SF).  It was presented to the county museum by Miss Bailey, the executrix of the owner's will, in 1881.<sup id="cite_ref-258" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-258">&#91;258&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Densities of white-tailed eagles have greatly increased in some parts of the range due to conservation efforts. Some threats still remain, notably illegal persecution by gamebird shooting and egg thieves in Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-rspb2_247-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rspb2-247">&#91;247&#93;</a></sup> In prime habitat in north Europe, distance between breeding pairs can be only 4&#160;km (2.5&#160;mi) (even as little as 1–2&#160;km&#160;[0.6–1.2&#160;mi] locally) such as in Norway, density at one Polish forest were even reported at 6–7 in each square kilometer.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_9-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> In the late 1990s, it was estimated that <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> held as many as 5,000-7,000 pairs, with around 175 pairs in <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a> and almost 3,500 pairs in <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>, led by <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a> (1,500+ pairs), <a href="/wiki/European_Russia" title="European Russia">European Russia</a> (900-1,100), <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a> (180-240), <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a> (140-150) and <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a> (100-150).<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_9-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> The largest population in Europe is found along the coast of <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-iucn_1-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iucn-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-birds.poland.pl_259-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-birds.poland.pl-259">&#91;259&#93;</a></sup> The Norwegian population in 2008 was claimed to have stood at 9,000–11,000 pairs, much larger than prior estimates, and indeed this may refer to the total number of individuals rather than total breeding pairs.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_9-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThe_Nature_of_Scotland49_17-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThe_Nature_of_Scotland49-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Even in the early 1980s, Norway maintained a population greater than all other European white-tailed eagle populations combined.<sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> In isolation, <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a> holds merely 10-30 pairs.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_9-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Over 500 winter in Japan, mainly Hokkaido, but this island may have only 20 pairs breeding.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_9-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Strong increases were recorded in <a href="/wiki/Croatia" title="Croatia">Croatia</a>, with no less than 135 breeding pairs estimated in Croatia by 2009, up from only 25–30 in 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-Radović_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Radović-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-260" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-260">&#91;260&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Beyond the threat of chemical poisonings, a new threat from <a href="/wiki/Wind_turbine" title="Wind turbine">wind turbines</a> is emerging with significant mortality (considerably in excess of the area's population productivity) occurring at the <a href="/wiki/Sm%C3%B8la_Wind_Farm" title="Smøla Wind Farm">Smøla Wind Farm</a> in Norway.<sup id="cite_ref-BirdLife_261-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BirdLife-261">&#91;261&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-fatbirder_262-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fatbirder-262">&#91;262&#93;</a></sup> From 2005 to 2010, 36 birds killed by wind farm on isle of <a href="/wiki/Sm%C3%B8la" title="Smøla">Smøla</a>, including 4 of 45 recent radio-tagged fledglings. Breeding attempts within 500–1,000&#160;m (1,600–3,300&#160;ft) of the wind farm were considerably reduced in success. The white-tailed eagles of the region seem to have no behavioral avoidance capabilities, as in many raptors, since the blades are not visible at close range.<sup id="cite_ref-263" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-263">&#91;263&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-264" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-264">&#91;264&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-265" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-265">&#91;265&#93;</a></sup> In a recovering population in north-east Germany, a trade-off between the distance to neighbouring breeding pairs and to the nearest water body (the birds' favoured foraging habitat) was found. This indicates that pairs may be increasingly selecting suboptimal habitats to reduce competition as the population increases, and has implications in the management of such populations.<sup id="cite_ref-266" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-266">&#91;266&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>White-tailed eagles have apparently re-established themselves as a native breeding species in numerous countries: <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austria</a> (now breeding in extreme northwestern portions), <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a> (where broadly re-established as breeders), the <a href="/wiki/Czech_Republic" title="Czech Republic">Czech Republic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Slovakia" title="Slovakia">Slovakia</a> (scattered pairs in both now with reintroductions factoring in the Czech Republic), <a href="/wiki/Hungary" title="Hungary">Hungary</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bulgaria" title="Bulgaria">Bulgaria</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-iucn_1-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iucn-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Love_3-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Love-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> In Denmark (excluding Greenland where the species never was extirpated as a breeder and there are 150–200 pairs in the south<sup id="cite_ref-DOFNorthGL_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DOFNorthGL-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-267" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-267">&#91;267&#93;</a></sup>), in just 16 years, the breeding population increased from none to at least 37 pairs by 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-Ehmsen_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ehmsen-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> In 2019, about 130 young were successfully reared by 80 pairs in Denmark (another 5 pairs did not fledge any young), far surpassing the initial goals of the species' recovery program in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-268" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-268">&#91;268&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-269" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-269">&#91;269&#93;</a></sup> In Hungary, re-establishment (starting from none in the 1970s) has also been a success, where 114 out of 166 breeding pairs by 2007 were successful producing altogether 182 fledged young. Wintering Hungarian population may now reach about a thousand eagles.<sup id="cite_ref-270" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-270">&#91;270&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Lithuania" title="Lithuania">Lithuania</a>, back in 1985 no pairs were known to have bred but established pairs numbered 90 by 2007 and swelled to 120 by 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-271" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-271">&#91;271&#93;</a></sup> On 22 May 2006, it was announced that a pair of white-tailed eagles breeding in the <a href="/wiki/Oostvaardersplassen" title="Oostvaardersplassen">Oostvaardersplassen</a> nature reserve in the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a> had arrived on their own, not as a reintroduction. This was the first time the bird has bred in the Netherlands in living memory. In 2007, 2008 and 2009 the eagles returned to their nest.<sup id="cite_ref-webcam_272-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-webcam-272">&#91;272&#93;</a></sup> In 2010, it turned out that the white-tailed eagle was also breeding in the Zwarte Meer nature district and in the Lauwersmeer area.<sup id="cite_ref-Janssen_273-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Janssen-273">&#91;273&#93;</a></sup> There is also a confirmed case of breeding white-tailed eagles in the <a href="/wiki/De_Biesbosch" class="mw-redirect" title="De Biesbosch">Biesbosch</a>. Currently (2017), there are around ten pairs breeding spread over Dutch country.
</p><p>Studies of <a href="/wiki/Microsatellite_(genetics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Microsatellite (genetics)">microsatellite</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrial_DNA" title="Mitochondrial DNA">mitochondrial DNA</a> in white-tailed eagles from north-central Europe have shown that the recovering European population has retained appreciable amounts of <a href="/wiki/Genetic_diversity" title="Genetic diversity">genetic diversity</a>, implying low risk of <a href="/wiki/Inbreeding_depression" title="Inbreeding depression">inbreeding depression</a> (a serious concern in species with low <a href="/wiki/Population_density" title="Population density">population density</a>). Therefore, recovery of this formerly endangered species is a true success story for nature conservation. The story also shows how local protection of a species can be successful and important for preserving the species' evolutionary potential.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHailerHelanderFolkestadGanusevich2006_274-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHailerHelanderFolkestadGanusevich2006-274">&#91;274&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHailer2006_275-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHailer2006-275">&#91;275&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHailerGautschiHelander2005_276-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHailerGautschiHelander2005-276">&#91;276&#93;</a></sup> In total, as of 2013, the <a href="/wiki/IUCN" class="mw-redirect" title="IUCN">IUCN</a> estimated the world population of white-tailed eagles at 20,000-49,999 individuals.<sup id="cite_ref-iucn_1-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iucn-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Heraldry">Heraldry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=White-tailed_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Heraldry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>White-tailed eagle are prominent in ancient Saxon folkore and artwork with many landmarks named after the species.
It is believed to be the White eagle shown in the <a href="/wiki/Coat_of_arms_of_Germany" title="Coat of arms of Germany">German Coat of arms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coat_of_arms_of_Poland" title="Coat of arms of Poland">Polish coat of arms</a> and in the <a href="/wiki/Coat_of_arms_of_Serbia" title="Coat of arms of Serbia">Serbian coat of arms</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-birds.poland.pl_259-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-birds.poland.pl-259">&#91;259&#93;</a></sup> The sea eagle is often blazoned grasping a fish (usually a <a href="/wiki/Esox" title="Esox">pike</a>) in its talons, distinguishing it from an ordinary eagle.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prehistory">Prehistory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=White-tailed_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Prehistory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>On <a href="/wiki/Orkney" title="Orkney">Orkney</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>, sea eagle bones have been found in 6000-year-old burial mounds, among them the <a href="/wiki/Tomb_of_the_Eagles" title="Tomb of the Eagles">Tomb of the Eagles</a>, suggesting that the birds were revered by the prehistoric people there, a belief strengthened by the Pictish stone carvings of sea eagles from Orkney.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThe_Nature_of_Scotland49_17-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThe_Nature_of_Scotland49-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Cut marks have been found in white-tailed eagle talons in <a href="/wiki/Krapina" title="Krapina">Krapina</a>, suggesting <a href="/wiki/Neanderthal" title="Neanderthal">Neanderthals</a>' use of <a href="/wiki/Jewellery" title="Jewellery">jewellery</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-PLOS_277-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PLOS-277">&#91;277&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Folklore">Folklore</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=White-tailed_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Folklore">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Shetland_Isles" class="mw-redirect" title="Shetland Isles">Shetland Isles</a>, Scotland, fishermen believed that as soon as a sea eagle appeared fish would rise to the surface, belly up; this led to some fishermen using eagle fat, smeared on their bait, to increase their catch.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThe_Nature_of_Scotland49_17-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThe_Nature_of_Scotland49-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-webcam-272"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-webcam_272-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The Dutch national forestry, which owns the reserve, has put up a webcam trained on the nesting eagles <a rel="nofollow" class="external autonumber" href="http://www.staatsbosbeheer.nl/actueel/zeearend/default.asp?id=%7B8301F776-F5D0-46A9-8432-99B08D34A3AC%7D">[1]</a> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20070310162650/http://www.staatsbosbeheer.nl/actueel/zeearend/default.asp?id=%7B8301F776-F5D0-46A9-8432-99B08D34A3AC%7D">Archived</a> March 10, 2007, at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a></span>

<dl><dt>Identification</dt></dl>
